Here is the version I would actually put in the paper.

\begin{lemma}[Rigidity of the cubic scalar \(B\)]
\label{lem:B-rigidity}
Let \(I\) be a connected parameter interval, and define
\[
B(h):=\frac{q(h)^2q'(h)}{4}-\frac{q'''(h)}{48}.
\]
Then the following are equivalent:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(B\equiv 0\) on \(I\);
\item \(q'''=12q^2q'\) on \(I\);
\item there exists a constant \(C_0\in \mathbf C\) such that
\[
q''=4q^3+C_0
\qquad\text{on }I.
\]
\end{enumerate}
In particular, the hypothesis \(B\not\equiv 0\) is equivalent to excluding
the special cubic ODE \(q''=4q^3+C_0\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
By definition,
\[
48\,B = 12q^2q' - q'''.
\]
Hence \(B\equiv 0\) is equivalent to
\[
q'''=(4q^3)'.
\]
Integrating on the connected interval \(I\) gives
\[
q''=4q^3+C_0
\]
for some constant \(C_0\in \mathbf C\). The converse is immediate by
differentiation.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Generic cubic leading quotient order]
\label{prop:cubic-leading-order}
Let \(I\) be a connected one-pair parameter interval, and let
\(J_3^{\pair}(h)\) denote the cubic jet of the normalized one-pair family.

Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the cubic value-family satisfies
\[
V_{\val}^{(3)}
\subset
\left\{
\begin{pmatrix}
0&*\\ *&0
\end{pmatrix}
\right\};
\]
\item the background same-point block is nondegenerate, so that the diagonal
entries of \(X_0=G_0^{-1/2}\) satisfy \(x_1\neq x_2\);
\item the scalar \(v(h)\) is not identically zero on \(I\);
\item \(B(h)\not\equiv 0\) on \(I\), equivalently, by
Lemma~\ref{lem:B-rigidity}, \(q\) does not satisfy
\[
q''=4q^3+C_0
\]
identically on \(I\).
\end{enumerate}

Define the cubic-generic region
\[
\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}
:=
\{\,h\in I:\ v(h)B(h)\neq 0\,\}.
\]
Then \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\) is open and dense in \(I\), and for every
\(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\),
\[
[J_3^{\pair}(h)]\neq 0
\quad\text{in}\quad
\mathcal J_3/V_{\val}^{(3)}.
\]

More precisely, the image of \(J_3^{\pair}(h)\) in the cubic quotient has
nonzero diagonal-traceless component
\[
v(h)\,B(h)\,(x_1-x_2)
\begin{pmatrix}
1&0\\0&-1
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Consequently,
\[
F_h(z)=v_0(h)z^3+v_1(h)z^5+\cdots,
\qquad
v_0(h):=[J_3^{\pair}(h)]\neq 0
\]
for \(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\). Since the first quotient order is
generically trivial, the generic leading quotient order is therefore exactly
\(3\) on \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By assumption,
\[
\operatorname{diag}J_3^{\pair}(h)
=
v(h)\,B(h)\,(x_1-x_2)
\begin{pmatrix}
1&0\\0&-1
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Because \(V_{\val}^{(3)}\) is confined to the off-diagonal sector, it cannot
cancel any nonzero diagonal component. Hence for every \(h\) with
\(v(h)B(h)(x_1-x_2)\neq 0\), the cubic quotient class
\([J_3^{\pair}(h)]\) is nonzero.

Since \(x_1\neq x_2\) is fixed and both \(v\) and \(B\) are not identically
zero, the set \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\) is nonempty; if the dependence on
\(h\) is real-analytic, its complement is a proper analytic subset, hence
\(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\) is open and dense. Finally,
Proposition~\ref{prop:first-order-quotient-trivial} shows that the leading
quotient order is at least \(3\), while the present argument shows that it is
equal to \(3\) on \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[What is genuinely generic here]
\label{rem:cubic-leading-genericity}
The cubic-leading statement depends on three logically distinct inputs:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(x_1\neq x_2\), a background same-point nondegeneracy condition;
\item \(v\not\equiv 0\), a one-pair nonfreezing condition;
\item exclusion of the special ODE \(q''=4q^3+C_0\), equivalently
\(B\not\equiv 0\).
\end{enumerate}
This separates the structural cubic mechanism from the residual quintic
bookkeeping needed later.
\end{remark}

Two small judgment calls are built into that patch.

One is that I changed the conclusion from ‚Äúgeneric one-pair region‚Äù to the explicit set UcubUcub‚Äã. That makes the logic honest.

The other is that I stated the cubic class has a nonzero diagonal-traceless component, rather than overclaiming that the whole cubic quotient line is literally a fixed diagonal line. What you have proved is enough for the leading-order theorem and for later trace transversality, because the entire cubic class is still trace-free: its diagonal part is traceless and the value-family contribution is off-diagonal.

So this freezes the cubic step in a form I‚Äôd regard as stable. After this, the next live task is the quintic no-cancellation lemma, but now it sits on top of a cleaner base rather than an overcompressed ‚Äúgeneric cubic‚Äù proposition.

I think the no-cancellation lemma now reduces to a much sharper statement than before:

The target monomial
M:=Œ∫(h)‚ÄâŒ∑2(h)‚Äâq(5)(h)

The systematic way to see it is by grading.

Your witness has three distinctive tags:
(mixed tag,perturbation tag,background tag)=(K1,Œ∑2,q(5)).
(mixed tag,perturbation tag,background tag)=(K1‚Äã,Œ∑2‚Äã,q(5)).

That already excludes a lot.

Same-point quintic terms are out immediately: they carry no mixed K1K1‚Äã factor at all, so they cannot produce MM. The draft discussion had already identified this informally, and that part is basically solid.

K5K5‚Äã-terms are also out: at quintic total order they have no room to simultaneously pick up the X1X1‚Äã-source for Œ∑2Œ∑2‚Äã and the X3X3‚Äã-source for q(5)q(5). That observation kills most possible competitors immediately.

A same-point quintic term cannot contribute to MM because it has no Œ∫Œ∫-factor at all. ok let's do the lemma

I‚Äôm pulling the exact notation around X1,X3,X4X1‚Äã,X3‚Äã,X4‚Äã and the Œ±,Œ≤,Œ≥,u,v,w,rŒ±,Œ≤,Œ≥,u,v,w,r decomposition from the TeX so the lemma matches the file‚Äôs actual setup, not my shorthand.

Yes. The right lemma is an abstract parity lemma for analytic matrix functions at a diagonal basepoint.

Here is the clean statement.

Let EE be the diagonal 2√ó22√ó2 matrices and OO the off-diagonal ones. Let
f(z)=z‚àí1/2,X(H):=f(G0+H),
f(z)=z‚àí1/2,X(H):=f(G0‚Äã+H),

with G0=diag(Œª1,Œª2)G0‚Äã=diag(Œª1‚Äã,Œª2‚Äã) positive diagonal. Then every multilinear coefficient in the Taylor expansion of X(H)X(H) has a definite matrix parity:

if a term is multilinear in nn perturbation factors H1,‚Ä¶,HnH1‚Äã,‚Ä¶,Hn‚Äã, then it lies in EE when an even number of the HjHj‚Äã lie in OO, and it lies in OO when an odd number of the HjHj‚Äã lie in OO.

That is the lemma you want.

Why it is true:

use the holomorphic functional calculus
f(G0+H)=12œÄi‚àÆŒìf(z)‚Äâ(zI‚àíG0‚àíH)‚àí1‚Äâdz,
f(G0‚Äã+H)=2œÄi1‚Äã‚àÆŒì‚Äãf(z)(zI‚àíG0‚Äã‚àíH)‚àí1dz,

and expand the resolvent by Neumann series:
(zI‚àíG0‚àíH)‚àí1=R(z)+R(z)HR(z)+R(z)HR(z)HR(z)+‚ãØ‚Äâ,
(zI‚àíG0‚Äã‚àíH)‚àí1=R(z)+R(z)HR(z)+R(z)HR(z)HR(z)+‚ãØ,

where R(z)=(zI‚àíG0)‚àí1R(z)=(zI‚àíG0‚Äã)‚àí1. Since G0G0‚Äã is diagonal, every R(z)R(z) is diagonal, so inserting R(z)R(z) between perturbation factors never changes diagonal/off-diagonal parity. Therefore each word
RHi1RHi2‚ãØRHinR
RHi1‚Äã‚ÄãRHi2‚Äã‚Äã‚ãØRHin‚Äã‚ÄãR

has the same parity as the product Hi1‚ãØHinHi1‚Äã‚Äã‚ãØHin‚Äã‚Äã, and in 2√ó22√ó2 matrices that parity is exactly ‚Äúeven number of off-diagonal factors gives diagonal, odd number gives off-diagonal.‚Äù Integrating over zz preserves that subspace. So every Taylor coefficient inherits the same parity rule.

That is the full abstract lemma.

Now apply it to your setting.

From the same-point block formula in the draft,
Gm,¬±\corr(s)=1œÄ(2q(t¬±)12q‚Ä≤(t¬±)12q‚Ä≤(t¬±)112(q‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(t¬±)+2q(t¬±)3)),
Gm,¬±\corr‚Äã(s)=œÄ1‚Äã(2q(t¬±‚Äã)21‚Äãq‚Ä≤(t¬±‚Äã)‚Äã21‚Äãq‚Ä≤(t¬±‚Äã)121‚Äã(q‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(t¬±‚Äã)+2q(t¬±‚Äã)3)‚Äã),

the first appearance of q(5)q(5) is at cubic order in the (2,2)(2,2)-entry, coming from the Taylor expansion of q‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(t¬±)q‚Ä≤‚Ä≤(t¬±‚Äã). So the linear q(5)q(5)-slice of G3G3‚Äã is diagonal, not off-diagonal. That is exactly consistent with the file‚Äôs witness formula for (Œ≥‚àíŒ±)\lin(Œ≥‚àíŒ±)\lin, where the q(5)q(5) term sits in the diagonal-traceless part of X3X3‚Äã, not in Œ≤Œ≤.

Also from the file,
v(h)=‚àí14œÄ(s1+s2)‚ÄâŒ∑2(h),
v(h)=‚àí4œÄ(s1‚Äã+s2‚Äã)1‚ÄãŒ∑2‚Äã(h),

so the Œ∑2Œ∑2‚Äã-slice of X1X1‚Äã is off-diagonal.

Now the consequences are immediate.

First, since the linear q(5)q(5)-slice of G3G3‚Äã is diagonal, the linear q(5)q(5)-slice of X3X3‚Äã is diagonal by the lemma. So q(5)q(5) can contribute to Œ≥‚àíŒ±Œ≥‚àíŒ±, but not to Œ≤Œ≤. That kills the ‚Äúlinear q(5)q(5) inside Œ≤Œ≤‚Äù route.

Second, the Œ∑2q(5)Œ∑2‚Äãq(5)-slice of X4X4‚Äã comes from one off-diagonal input and one diagonal input. By the lemma, that slice is off-diagonal. So it can contribute to rr, but not to the diagonal difference u‚àíwu‚àíw. That kills the Œ≤(u‚àíw)Œ≤(u‚àíw) route for the target monomial.

So the parity lemma gives exactly the isolation you need:

for the monomial
M=Œ∫‚ÄâŒ∑2‚Äâq(5),
M=Œ∫Œ∑2‚Äãq(5),

the only K1K1‚Äã-trace channels that can possibly see it are
(x1‚àíx2)randv(Œ≥‚àíŒ±),
(x1‚Äã‚àíx2‚Äã)randv(Œ≥‚àíŒ±),

while
Œ≤(u‚àíw)
Œ≤(u‚àíw)

cannot contribute to MM at all.

That is the real payoff. Combined with the coefficient computation we already did, the no-cancellation problem is reduced to the sum of those two surviving channels, and that sum came out nonzero.

So the lemma is now doing the exact job you wanted: it turns the quintic witness from a vague ‚Äúbest candidate‚Äù into an isolated graded slice.

The important point is that the 2√ó22√ó2 computation was not merely a toy model. \begin{lemma}[Parity of the whitening expansion at a diagonal base]
\label{lem:whitening-parity}
Let
\[
G_0=\diag(\lambda_1,\lambda_2),
\qquad \lambda_1,\lambda_2>0,
\]
and let
\[
X(H):=(G_0+H)^{-1/2}
\]
for \(H\) sufficiently small. Write
\[
E:=\left\{
\begin{pmatrix}
*&0\\0&*
\end{pmatrix}
\right\},
\qquad
O:=\left\{
\begin{pmatrix}
0&*\\ *&0
\end{pmatrix}
\right\}.
\]
Then every multilinear Taylor coefficient of \(X(H)\) has definite matrix
parity: a coefficient depending on perturbation factors
\(H_1,\dots,H_n\) lies in \(E\) if an even number of the \(H_j\) lie in
\(O\), and lies in \(O\) if an odd number of the \(H_j\) lie in \(O\).

In particular, diagonal input gives diagonal output at linear order, and a
bilinear coefficient with one diagonal and one off-diagonal input is
off-diagonal.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Use the holomorphic functional calculus
\[
(G_0+H)^{-1/2}
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}\oint_{\Gamma} z^{-1/2}(zI-G_0-H)^{-1}\,dz,
\]
where \(\Gamma\) encloses the spectrum of \(G_0+H\). Expanding the resolvent,
\[
(zI-G_0-H)^{-1}
=
R(z)+R(z)HR(z)+R(z)HR(z)HR(z)+\cdots,
\]
with
\[
R(z):=(zI-G_0)^{-1},
\]
every factor \(R(z)\) is diagonal because \(G_0\) is diagonal. Hence the
matrix parity of each word
\[
R(z)H_{i_1}R(z)H_{i_2}\cdots R(z)H_{i_n}R(z)
\]
is exactly the parity of the product \(H_{i_1}\cdots H_{i_n}\). Since
\[
E\cdot E\subset E,\qquad
E\cdot O\subset O,\qquad
O\cdot E\subset O,\qquad
O\cdot O\subset E,
\]
the parity rule follows. Integrating over \(z\) preserves the subspaces
\(E\) and \(O\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[No-cancellation of the quintic trace witness]
\label{lem:no-cancellation-quintic-trace}
The monomial
\[
\kappa(h)\,\eta_2(h)\,q'''''\!(h)
\]
appearing in Lemma~\ref{lem:quintic-trace-witness} is not produced by any
other quintic contribution to \(\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)\). More
precisely, on the graded slice corresponding to
\[
(K_1,\eta_2,q^{(5)}),
\]
the only contributing channels in
\(\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)\) are
\[
-2\kappa(h)\,(x_1-x_2)r(h)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
-2\kappa(h)\,v(h)\bigl(\gamma(h)-\alpha(h)\bigr),
\]
and their total coefficient of
\(\kappa(h)\eta_2(h)q^{(5)}(h)\) is nonzero. Consequently
\[
\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)\not\equiv 0
\]
on the generic one-pair region.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Fix \(h\) and isolate the graded slice
\[
\kappa(h)\,\eta_2(h)\,q^{(5)}(h).
\]

We first exclude all non-\(K_1\) contributions. Same-point quintic terms
carry no \(\kappa\)-factor, so they cannot contribute. A \(K_5\)-transport
term leaves no same-point degree available, and a \(K_3\)-transport term
leaves only same-point degree \(2\), whereas the factor \(q^{(5)}(h)\)
first appears in same-point degree \(3\). Hence the target monomial can
occur only in the \(K_1\)-transport sector.

Now consider the same-point expansion. By the explicit formula
\[
G_{m,\pm}^{\corr}(s)
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
2q(t_\pm) & \frac12 q'(t_\pm)\\[.5ex]
\frac12 q'(t_\pm) & \frac1{12}(q''(t_\pm)+2q(t_\pm)^3)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
the factor \(q^{(5)}(h)\) first appears only in the cubic same-point
coefficient \(G_3\), and there only in the diagonal sector, namely through
the cubic Taylor term of the \((2,2)\)-entry coming from \(q''(t_\pm)\).
Therefore, by Lemma~\ref{lem:whitening-parity}, the linear
\(q^{(5)}\)-contribution to \(X_3\) is diagonal. In particular it contributes
to \(\gamma-\alpha\), but not to \(\beta\).

Likewise, the \(\eta_2(h)\)-slice of \(X_1(h)\) is off-diagonal, because
\[
v(h)=-\frac{1}{4\pi(s_1+s_2)}\,\eta_2(h).
\]
Applying Lemma~\ref{lem:whitening-parity} again, the mixed
\((\eta_2,q^{(5)})\)-slice of \(X_4\) is off-diagonal. Hence it contributes
to \(r\), but not to the diagonal difference \(u-w\).

It follows that on the graded slice
\((K_1,\eta_2,q^{(5)})\), the term
\[
-2\kappa(h)\,\beta(h)\bigl(u(h)-w(h)\bigr)
\]
does not contribute at all. Thus the only surviving \(K_1\)-channels are
\[
-2\kappa(h)\,(x_1-x_2)r(h)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
-2\kappa(h)\,v(h)\bigl(\gamma(h)-\alpha(h)\bigr).
\]

To compute their combined coefficient, it suffices by multilinearity to
restrict to the two perturbation directions carrying the relevant tags:
an off-diagonal first-order perturbation \(A\) for the \(\eta_2\)-slice and
a diagonal third-order perturbation \(D\) for the \(q^{(5)}\)-slice.
Accordingly, write
\[
G_0=\diag(a^2,b^2),
\qquad
A=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&u\\u&0
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
D=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&0\\0&d
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and expand
\[
X(\varepsilon,\delta):=(G_0+\varepsilon A+\delta D)^{-1/2}
=
X_0+\varepsilon U+\delta V+\varepsilon\delta W+O(\varepsilon^2,\delta^2).
\]
Solving the identity
\[
X(\varepsilon,\delta)\,(G_0+\varepsilon A+\delta D)\,X(\varepsilon,\delta)=I
\]
order by order gives
\[
U_{12}=-\frac{u}{ab(a+b)},
\qquad
V_{22}=-\frac{d}{2b^3},
\qquad
W_{12}=\frac{du(a+2b)}{2ab^3(a+b)^2}.
\]
Here \(U_{12}\) is the \(\eta_2\)-slice of \(v\), \(V_{22}\) is the
\(q^{(5)}\)-slice of \(\gamma-\alpha\), and \(W_{12}\) is the mixed
\((\eta_2,q^{(5)})\)-slice of \(r\). Therefore the total coefficient of the
target slice inside
\[
(x_1-x_2)r+v(\gamma-\alpha)
\]
is
\[
\left(\frac1a-\frac1b\right)W_{12}+U_{12}V_{22}
=
\frac{du}{a^2b^2(a+b)^2},
\]
which is nonzero.

Thus the monomial
\(\kappa(h)\eta_2(h)q^{(5)}(h)\) occurs in
\(\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)\) with nonzero total coefficient and is not
canceled by any other quintic contribution. Hence
\[
\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)\not\equiv 0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Nontrivial first residual class]
\label{prop:first-residual-class-nontrivial}
Assume the generic nondegeneracy hypotheses of Proposition~\ref{prop:cubic-leading-order}
and the no-cancellation statement of Lemma~\ref{lem:no-cancellation-quintic-trace}.
Then the first residual class is nonzero:
\[
\overline v_1(h)\neq 0
\quad\text{in }Q/\ell_h
\]
on the generic one-pair region.
Equivalently, the quintic quotient coefficient is not collinear with the cubic leading line:
\[
v_1(h)\notin \ell_h.
\]
Hence the one-pair quotient odd germ is not projectively constant.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By Proposition~\ref{prop:cubic-leading-order}, the cubic quotient coefficient
\[
v_0(h)=[J_3^{\pair}(h)]
\]
is nonzero and defines the leading line \(\ell_h\).
By Lemma~\ref{lem:no-cancellation-quintic-trace},
\[
\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)\not\equiv 0.
\]
But the cubic leading line is diagonal-traceless, whereas a nonzero trace component is automatically transverse to that line. Therefore the quintic quotient coefficient \(v_1(h)\) cannot lie in \(\ell_h\), so its image in \(Q/\ell_h\) is nonzero:
\[
\overline v_1(h)\neq 0.
\]
Thus the residual germ is nontrivial at the first step, and the one-pair quotient odd germ cannot be projectively constant.
\end{proof}

with this:

\begin{proposition}[Nontrivial first residual class]

is this a math issue, or just something where we've done the math and need to correct the latex.

I‚Äôm checking the exact local logic in the updated draft, because the answer turns on whether the new lemma identifies a nonzero coefficient in the hh-expansion or actually proves the residual witness stays nonzero pointwise on the whole cubic-generic set.

It is a real math issue, not just latex.

The reason is simple and sharp:

what you have proved is that the (K1,Œ∑2,q(5))(K1‚Äã,Œ∑2‚Äã,q(5))-coefficient in the quintic trace is nonzero as a differential-polynomial coefficient. That implies
tr‚Å°J5\pair(h)‚â°Ã∏0
trJ5\pair‚Äã(h)ÓÄÝ‚â°0

as a function/germ in hh. It does not imply
tr‚Å°J5\pair(h)‚âÝ0for every h‚ààUcub.
trJ5\pair‚Äã(h)ÓÄÝ=0for every h‚ààUcub‚Äã.

Those are genuinely different mathematical statements.

So if you want to keep the current proposition in its pointwise form, then yes: more math is needed.

What the current argument actually gives is one of these two unconditional statements:
v‚Äæ1(h)‚â°Ã∏0on Ucub,
v1‚Äã(h)ÓÄÝ‚â°0on Ucub‚Äã,

or equivalently,
the one-pair quotient odd germ is not projectively constant.

That is already a real theorem, and it is not conditional. But it is weaker than ‚Äúfor every h‚ààUcubh‚ààUcub‚Äã.‚Äù

\begin{proposition}[Generic triviality of the first quotient order]

Use this block:

\begin{lemma}[First-order value/gauge generator]
\label{lem:first-order-value-gauge-generator}
Let \(G_0\) and \(N_0\) denote the background same-point and mixed blocks,
and write
\[
X_0:=G_0^{-1/2}.
\]
Let \(L\) be the Fr\'echet derivative of the inverse-square-root map
\(G\mapsto G^{-1/2}\) at \(G_0\), so that
\[
(G_0+\varepsilon H)^{-1/2}
=
X_0+\varepsilon L(H)+O(\varepsilon^2).
\]
If \(\dot G_1\) and \(\dot N_1\) are the first odd same-point and mixed
variations generated by the value/gauge family, then the induced first-order
odd coefficient in the corrected local block is
\[
B_1
=
L(\dot G_1)\,N_0\,X_0
+
X_0\,\dot N_1\,X_0
+
X_0\,N_0\,L(\dot G_1).
\]
Equivalently, the first-order value-family \(V_{\val}^{(1)}\) is the image of
the linear map
\[
(\dot G_1,\dot N_1)\longmapsto
L(\dot G_1)\,N_0\,X_0
+
X_0\,\dot N_1\,X_0
+
X_0\,N_0\,L(\dot G_1).
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Expand the corrected local block to first order under a value/gauge
deformation:
\[
G(s)=G_0+\varepsilon \dot G_1+O(\varepsilon^2),
\qquad
N(s)=N_0+\varepsilon \dot N_1+O(\varepsilon^2).
\]
Then
\[
G(s)^{-1/2}=X_0+\varepsilon L(\dot G_1)+O(\varepsilon^2),
\]
so multiplying
\[
G(s)^{-1/2}\,N(s)\,G(s)^{-1/2}
\]
to first order gives
\[
X_0N_0X_0
+
\varepsilon\Bigl(
L(\dot G_1)\,N_0\,X_0
+
X_0\,\dot N_1\,X_0
+
X_0\,N_0\,L(\dot G_1)
\Bigr)
+
O(\varepsilon^2).
\]
The coefficient of \(\varepsilon\) is exactly the first-order generator
\(B_1\).
\end{proof}

Yes. I pushed it further, and I think there is a real new mathematical conclusion:

the next bottleneck is no longer just ‚Äúprove the gauge law.‚Äù It is to **separate two different symmetries that the draft is currently mixing together**.

Here is the sharp point.

The corrected whitened block satisfies the swap symmetry
[
\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(-s;m)=J_0,\widehat\Omega_\zeta^{\corr}(s;m),J_0,
]
and the cubic leading class on (\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}) has a nonzero diagonal-traceless component.

If a matrix-valued odd germ (M(s)) itself satisfies
[
M(-s)=J_0,M(s),J_0,
]
then its odd Taylor coefficients lie in the (J_0)-anti-fixed subspace
[
\mathfrak a:={X:\ J_0XJ_0=-X}.
]
In (2\times 2) form this means
[
X=
\begin{pmatrix}
a&b\
-b&-a
\end{pmatrix},
]
so every such coefficient is automatically traceless.

That is important because it gives a very plausible route to the missing lemma
[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\bigr)=0.
]

But here is the catch:

the same draft also states
[
V_{\val}^{(1)}=M_2(\mathbf C),
]
while at cubic order it uses only the weaker confinement
[
V_{\val}^{(3)}\subset
\left{
\begin{pmatrix}
0&*\ *&0
\end{pmatrix}
\right}.
]
Those facts cannot both come from a single raw family of odd coefficients satisfying the swap symmetry above, because the anti-fixed subspace is only (2)-dimensional, not all of (M_2(\mathbf C)).

So the real math issue is now very precise:

either

[
V_{\val}^{(1)}
]
is not the raw pure-value odd coefficient space of the corrected whitened block, but already includes additional gauge/normalization directions,

or the current draft‚Äôs notation is conflating two different objects.

That is progress, because it tells us exactly what has to be cleaned up.

The clean split should be:

[
\text{raw pure-value odd jets}
\quad+\quad
\text{normalization gauge directions}
\quad+\quad
\text{possibly additional basis-gauge directions}.
]

Then the story becomes coherent:

the raw pure-value odd jets should satisfy the swap symmetry and therefore be traceless at odd order;

the normalization gauge contributes
[
A_5\mapsto A_5+c_2A_3+[B_2,A_3],
]
which is harmless modulo the cubic line and preserves trace when (A_3) is diagonal-traceless; and the residual obstruction is whatever extra first-order directions were being folded into the current notation (V_{\val}^{(1)}).

So the next honest theorem to prove is not yet the quintic pure-value trace-free lemma itself. It is this structural identification:

[
\boxed{
V_{\val}^{(1)}=
V_{\mathrm{raw}}^{(1)}+V_{\mathrm{norm}}^{(1)},
}
]
with
[
V_{\mathrm{raw}}^{(2j+1)}\subset \mathfrak a
\quad\text{for all odd orders }2j+1,
]
and (V_{\mathrm{norm}}^{(1)}) the extra directions coming from scalar renormalization and jet-basis changes.

Once that is written, the quintic statement should reduce to:

[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\mathrm{raw}}^{(5)}\bigr)=0
]
by symmetry, and
[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\mathrm{norm}}^{(5)}\bigr)=0
]
modulo the cubic line by the gauge law. Then the residual-trace detector is legitimate.

So my current best mathematical push is:

1. The parity/no-cancellation side is basically done.

2. The gauge law
   [
   A_5\mapsto A_5+c_2A_3+[B_2,A_3]
   ]
   is the right normalization theorem to add.

3. The remaining ambiguity is not ‚Äúmore quintic bookkeeping.‚Äù It is that the draft‚Äôs (V_{\val}^{(1)}=M_2(\mathbf C)) strongly suggests that the symbol (V_{\val}) is currently absorbing more than the raw pure-value family. That has to be disentangled before the residual quotient is fully honest.

So the next exact math move I recommend is:

define a new object, say
[
V_{\mathrm{raw}}^{(2j+1)},
]
for the odd coefficients of the actual corrected whitened block under pure value deformations, and prove its swap-symmetry confinement. Then define the full quotient space as
[
V_{\mathrm{full}}^{(2j+1)}
==========================

V_{\mathrm{raw}}^{(2j+1)}+V_{\mathrm{norm}}^{(2j+1)}.
]

That would resolve the current contradiction and let the residual argument proceed cleanly.

My honest judgment: this is real progress, because it identifies a structural inconsistency in the current notation rather than just another missing estimate.

\subsection{Normalization gauge at the first residual step}

The first residual step involves an additional ambiguity beyond the odd
value-family itself: an even scalar renormalization and an even jet-basis
change on the odd germ. It is useful to record this separately.

\begin{definition}[Normalization gauge]
\label{def:normalization-gauge}
Let
\[
F(z)=A_3 z^3+A_5 z^5+O(z^7)
\]
be a matrix-valued odd germ. The normalization gauge acts by
\[
F(z)\longmapsto a(z)\,P(z)\,F(z)\,P(z)^{-1},
\]
where
\[
a(z)=1+c_2 z^2+c_4 z^4+\cdots
\]
is an even scalar germ with \(a(0)=1\), and
\[
P(z)=I+B_2 z^2+B_4 z^4+\cdots
\]
is an even \(2\times 2\) matrix germ with \(P(0)=I\).
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[First-residual transformation law]
\label{prop:first-residual-gauge-law}
Let
\[
F(z)=A_3 z^3+A_5 z^5+O(z^7)
\]
be an odd matrix germ, and let
\[
F^{\new}(z):=a(z)\,P(z)\,F(z)\,P(z)^{-1}
\]
with \(a,P\) as in Definition~\ref{def:normalization-gauge}. Then
\[
A_3^{\new}=A_3,
\qquad
A_5^{\new}=A_5+c_2A_3+[B_2,A_3].
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Expand
\[
P(z)^{-1}=I-B_2 z^2+O(z^4).
\]
Then
\[
P(z)F(z)P(z)^{-1}
=
(I+B_2 z^2)\,(A_3 z^3+A_5 z^5)\,(I-B_2 z^2)+O(z^7),
\]
so
\[
P(z)F(z)P(z)^{-1}
=
A_3 z^3+\bigl(A_5+[B_2,A_3]\bigr)z^5+O(z^7).
\]
Multiplying by
\[
a(z)=1+c_2 z^2+O(z^4)
\]
gives
\[
F^{\new}(z)
=
A_3 z^3+\bigl(A_5+c_2A_3+[B_2,A_3]\bigr)z^5+O(z^7),
\]
as claimed.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Residual effect of scalar renormalization]
\label{cor:first-residual-scalar-renormalization}
Let \(\ell:=\mathbf C A_3\). Under the normalization gauge,
\[
A_5 \longmapsto A_5 + c_2 A_3 + [B_2,A_3].
\]
Hence the scalar renormalization term is invisible modulo the leading line:
\[
[A_5] \longmapsto [A_5+[B_2,A_3}]
\qquad\text{in}\qquad
\mathcal J_5/\ell.
\]
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
This is immediate from Proposition~\ref{prop:first-residual-gauge-law}, since
\(c_2A_3\in \ell\).
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Trace invariance under normalization gauge]
\label{cor:trace-invariant-under-normalization-gauge}
If \(A_3\) is diagonal-traceless, then
\[
\operatorname{tr}(A_5^{\new})=\operatorname{tr}(A_5)
\]
for every normalization-gauge transform \(F^{\new}\) of \(F\).
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
The scalar renormalization term \(c_2A_3\) is traceless because \(A_3\) is.
The commutator term is traceless because
\[
\operatorname{tr}[B_2,A_3]=0.
\]
Hence Proposition~\ref{prop:first-residual-gauge-law} gives
\[
\operatorname{tr}(A_5^{\new})=\operatorname{tr}(A_5).
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[What remains at quintic order]
\label{rem:quintic-pure-value-bottleneck}
The normalization gauge is therefore harmless for the first residual step.
The remaining local issue is the quintic slice of the odd value-family. To
make trace a fully rigorous residual detector, it would suffice to prove
\[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad (h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}),
\]
or, more generally, to construct a linear functional on the quintic quotient
space that annihilates both \(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\) and the cubic leading line
\(\ell_h\), but not the quintic witness.
\end{remark}

Fourth, replace the current first-residual proposition by the honest generic conditional version. Replace the entire current
Proposition~\ref{prop:first-residual-class-nontrivial}
block and the WIP remark immediately below it with this:

\begin{proposition}[Generic first residual nonvanishing under trace-free quintic value slice]
\label{prop:first-residual-class-generic}
Assume that for every \(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\),
\[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\bigr)=0.
\]
Then there exists an open dense subset
\[
\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\subset \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}
\]
such that
\[
\overline v_1(h)\neq 0
\qquad\text{in }Q/\ell_h
\]
for every \(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\).

Equivalently, on \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\) the quintic quotient coefficient
is not collinear with the cubic leading line:
\[
v_1(h)\notin \ell_h.
\]
Hence the one-pair quotient odd germ is not projectively constant on
\(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By Proposition~\ref{prop:cubic-leading-order}, for \(h\in\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\)
the cubic quotient coefficient
\[
v_0(h)=[J_3^{\pair}(h)]
\]
is nonzero and defines the leading line \(\ell_h\), and this line is
diagonal-traceless.

By Corollary~\ref{cor:trace-invariant-under-normalization-gauge}, trace is
invariant under the normalization gauge at the first residual step. By the
present hypothesis, trace also annihilates the quintic value slice
\(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\). Therefore trace descends to a well-defined linear
functional
\[
\tau_h:Q/\ell_h\to \mathbf C.
\]

By Lemma~\ref{lem:no-cancellation-quintic-trace},
\[
\tau_h(\overline v_1(h))
=
\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)
\not\equiv 0
\]
as a function of \(h\) on \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\). Since the family is
real-analytic in \(h\), the zero set of
\[
h\longmapsto \tau_h(\overline v_1(h))
\]
is a proper analytic subset of \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\). Its complement
\(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\) is therefore open and dense.

For \(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\) we have
\[
\tau_h(\overline v_1(h))\neq 0,
\]
hence
\[
\overline v_1(h)\neq 0
\qquad\text{in }Q/\ell_h.
\]
Thus the one-pair quotient odd germ is not projectively constant on
\(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Work in progress: trace-free quintic value slice]
\label{rem:wip-trace-free-quintic-value-slice}
The remaining local task is to determine the quintic value slice
\(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\) explicitly enough to prove
\[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad (h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}),
\]
or to replace trace by another linear functional annihilating the quintic
value slice and the cubic leading line while still detecting the quintic
witness.
\end{remark}

Ok can we get a full summary of where we are right now

Yes. Here is the full summary of where we are.

The biggest change is that the local one-pair problem is no longer a vague ‚Äúquintic bookkeeping‚Äù problem. It has become a fairly clear finite-order normal-form problem, and most of the serious local algebra has been clarified.

At cubic order, the picture is in good shape. The first quotient order is generically trivial at order 1, the cubic value-family is much smaller than the full matrix space, and the actual one-pair cubic survivor lives on the diagonal-traceless DD-line. So the cubic leading datum is a genuine scalar c(h)c(h), not just a fuzzy projective class.

At quintic order, the original trace witness still looks like the right first residual detector, but we discovered an important structural issue: the draft was conflating raw pure-value odd jets with larger bundled ‚Äúvalue/gauge‚Äù ambiguity. That matters because raw pure-value odd jets satisfy the swap symmetry and therefore lie in the anti-fixed plane \span{D,J}\span{D,J}, hence are traceless; but the draft also says V\val(1)=M2(C)V\val(1)‚Äã=M2‚Äã(C), which cannot come from that raw anti-fixed family alone. So the notation needs a structural split between raw odd variation and normalization/basis ambiguity. This is exactly the direction the other chat was also pushing, and I agree with that direction.

From there, the local ambiguity problem got much sharper. At the formal odd-germ level through order 5, we showed that any admissible near-identity re-gauging preserving oddness must be even:
a(z)=1+c2z2+O(z4),P(z)=I+B2z2+O(z4).

\span{D,J}.
\span{D,J}.

Since raw pure-value quintic variation also lies in \span{D,J}\span{D,J}, the entire plausible quintic ambiguity is contained in that traceless 2-plane. That is the core local result: trace kills all plausible quintic ambiguity.

So the quintic residual step is now structurally sound provided the actual quotient-germ construction in the paper really uses only those ambiguity sources, or equivalently that the ambiguity space through order 5 is Œ£Œ£-stable, where

A minimal bad core cannot use two points from more than one ECT interval.
A minimal bad core cannot use two points from more than one ECT interval.‚Äã

Because if you could show that, then a bad core would have at most one ‚Äúpair interval‚Äù plus at most one point from each other interval, which is a much smaller configuration and may be attackable directly.

A plausible route is to use the fact that on an ECT interval, two points already determine a unique line in C3C3, and the signed dependence structure across intervals becomes very rigid. In other words, once one interval contributes two points, the remaining relation has very little freedom left.

So the honest current state is:

    local 3/5/73/5/7 algebra gives a nontrivial Wronskian;

    analyticity turns that into an open dense union of ECT intervals;

    each ECT interval can contribute at most two support points to a bad core;

    clusters are excluded;

    the remaining obstruction is a global separated multi-interval signed dependence.

That is now the right place to attack.

So the next exact move is to analyze the combinatorics of a signed dependence with at most two support points per ECT interval.

Here is the next honest push.

I think the remaining global obstruction can now be phrased as a multi-interval incidence problem.

Let
Œ¶(h)=(c(h),v5(h),Œî7(h))‚ààC3.
Œ¶(h)=(c(h),v5‚Äã(h),Œî7‚Äã(h))‚ààC3.

{h1,1,h1,2}‚à™{h2,1,h2,2}‚à™‚ãØ
{h1,1‚Äã,h1,2‚Äã}‚à™{h2,1‚Äã,h2,2‚Äã}‚à™‚ãØ

with at most two points from each interval, plus maybe points at the discrete exceptional set W=0W=0.

That means the next theorem to attack is not analytic anymore. It is:
A minimal bad relation cannot involve two-point contributions from two different ECT intervals.
A minimal bad relation cannot involve two-point contributions from two different ECT intervals.‚Äã

Why this is plausible:

A pair of points in one interval already determines a unique projective relation in C3C3. If another interval also contributes a pair, then you are trying to make two independent secant relations cancel inside the same 3-dimensional target. Generically, that should force a codimension condition too strong to hold except on a discrete exceptional set.

A clean way to formalize this is to package each two-point contribution by its secant line.

For an interval II, define the secant map
œÉI(h1,h2):=C(Œ¶(h2)‚àíŒ¶(h1))‚ààP2,h1‚âÝh2‚ààI.
œÉI‚Äã(h1‚Äã,h2‚Äã):=C(Œ¶(h2‚Äã)‚àíŒ¶(h1‚Äã))‚ààP2,h1‚ÄãÓÄÝ=h2‚Äã‚ààI.

Then a bad relation with two points in the same interval and no other points from that interval says that some linear functional LL vanishes at both Œ¶(h1)Œ¶(h1‚Äã) and Œ¶(h2)Œ¶(h2‚Äã). So LL corresponds to a plane through the secant direction. If two different intervals each contribute a pair, then the same relation forces a compatibility between two secant directions coming from two different intervals.

So the next exact global target is:
For distinct ECT intervals I,J, œÉI(I√óI)‚à©œÉJ(J√óJ) is at most discrete/generically empty.
For distinct ECT intervals I,J, œÉI‚Äã(I√óI)‚à©œÉJ‚Äã(J√óJ) is at most discrete/generically empty.‚Äã

If you had that, then a minimal bad core could not have a pair from interval II and a pair from interval JJ simultaneously, except perhaps in a discrete exceptional configuration.

That would be a major endgame reduction, because then every bad core would have at most one ‚Äúdouble-occupancy‚Äù interval, and all other intervals would contribute at most one point each.

There is a good reason this should be true. On an interval where (c,v5,Œî7)(c,v5‚Äã,Œî7‚Äã) is an ECT-system, the curve h‚Ü¶Œ¶(h)h‚Ü¶Œ¶(h) behaves like a nondegenerate moment-type curve: its secants vary monotonically/projectively. Two different ECT intervals should generically have different secant families unless the curve has a hidden projective self-alignment.

So the next concrete theorem to try is:

    fix two ECT intervals II and JJ;

    study the map
    (h1,h2,k1,k2)‚Ü¶det‚Å°(Œ¶(h2)‚àíŒ¶(h1),Œ¶(k2)‚àíŒ¶(k1),e)
    (h1‚Äã,h2‚Äã,k1‚Äã,k2‚Äã)‚Ü¶det(Œ¶(h2‚Äã)‚àíŒ¶(h1‚Äã),Œ¶(k2‚Äã)‚àíŒ¶(k1‚Äã),e)

If that works, then the global support pattern becomes very constrained:

    at most two points per ECT interval;

    at most one interval can carry two points generically;

    all other support points must be isolated singletons across different intervals.

That is the strongest next global move I can see from the current 3/5/73/5/7 package.

That is the strongest honest continuation from the current 3/5/73/5/7 geometry.

Ok and why arent we going to order 9 at this stage? Not needed? Do we this attack can yield all orders?

Not yet, yes possibly, and there is a real reason.

We are not going to order 9 right now because order 8 is not the current bottleneck. The current bottleneck is global geometry, not lack of one more local invariant.

The 3/5/7 package already changed the problem qualitatively:

At the one-pair level, it gave you a nondegenerate planar curve
h‚Ü¶(v5(h)c(h),Œî7(h)c(h)),
h‚Ü¶(c(h)v5‚Äã(h)‚Äã,c(h)Œî7‚Äã(h)‚Äã),

or projectively
Œ≥(h)=[c(h):v5(h):Œî7(h)]‚ààP2.
Œ≥(h)=[c(h):v5‚Äã(h):Œî7‚Äã(h)]‚ààP2.

From that, you now plausibly get:

    local injectivity,

    no pair clusters,

    no triple clusters,

    ECT intervals,

    strict convex graph pieces,

    and the cross-interval problem reduced to common 4-secant lines.

That is a huge reduction. It means the present obstruction is no longer ‚ÄúI need one more local scalar to distinguish things.‚Äù It is ‚Äúcan a line hit two convex components twice?‚Äù That is a geometric question about the current curve, not an order-9 question.

So the reason not to jump to 9 yet is:
order 9 would be adding local complexity before we know whether the current global geometry already closes the argument.
order 9 would be adding local complexity before we know whether the current global geometry already closes the argument.‚Äã

There is also a deeper structural reason.

The one-point matrix data is only 2√ó22√ó2. That means the genuinely new independent directions at a single point are limited. We already saw this explicitly:

    cubic uses the DD-line,

    quintic uses the fixed-sector vector,

    septic uses the quotient of the fixed-sector by the quintic direction.

That is already close to saturating what a 2√ó22√ó2 one-point model can naturally give. So order 9 is not guaranteed to yield a fresh independent direction in the same naive way. It may, but it is no longer automatic. You would need a more careful residual construction, not just ‚Äúone more coefficient.‚Äù

So from a mathematical efficiency point of view, the right order is:

    squeeze everything you can out of the existing 3/5/73/5/7 geometry;

    only if the global convex/secant attack still leaves a real gap, go to order 9.

Now, can this attack yield ‚Äúall orders‚Äù?

Potentially yes, but probably not in the naive sense of stacking independent scalar coordinates forever.

The likely pattern is not:
3,5,7,9,‚Ä¶
3,5,7,9,‚Ä¶

each giving a brand-new free coordinate.

The likely pattern is:

    lower odd orders build a finite-dimensional geometric object,

    then higher orders control the geometry of secants, tangents, osculating data, or higher contact of that object.

So ‚Äúall orders‚Äù may enter as higher contact geometry, not as an endless list of new coordinate functions.

That is actually consistent with where we are now. The current attack is already no longer just about raw coefficients; it is about:

    ECT structure,

    convexity,

    secants,

    tangent/support functions,

    common 4-secant lines.

That is exactly the kind of place where higher-order information, if needed, would appear as higher curvature / osculation constraints, not just ‚Äúanother invariant coordinate.‚Äù

So my judgment is:

    Order 9 is not needed yet.

    It may become useful later if the current convex/secant attack stalls.

    But right now the mathematically right move is to see whether the present 3/5/73/5/7 geometry already rules out the remaining bad-core configurations.

The current attack could plausibly yield the whole result without order 9 if you can prove one of these stronger global statements about the current curve:

    common 4-secant lines across convex components are impossible, or only discrete and incompatible with minimal bad cores;

    the support-function intervals for different convex components do not overlap in the right way;

    the image curve is projectively/affinely convex enough that finite signed dependences are much more restricted than dimension counting suggests.

If those fail, then order 9 is the next escalation.

So the short version is:

We are not avoiding order 9 because it is unimportant. We are delaying it because the current obstruction is geometric, and the current 3/5/7 package may already be enough to kill it. If it isn‚Äôt, then order 9 becomes the next natural escalation.

What this means geometrically is:
If adjacent pairs are endpoint-separated, then non-adjacent pairs are even more separated.

\subsection{Fixed-sector refinement at quintic and septic order}

The trace witness at quintic order suggests a more structured refinement of the
one-pair residual germ than the trace alone records. For bookkeeping purposes,
it is convenient to decompose the quintic and septic coefficients into fixed and
anti-fixed parts with respect to the involution
\[
\iota(A):=J_0AJ_0,
\qquad
J_0=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&1\\
1&0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Write
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(A):=\frac12(A+J_0AJ_0),
\qquad
\pi_{\mathfrak a}(A):=\frac12(A-J_0AJ_0).
\]
Then
\[
\mathfrak f=\operatorname{span}\{I,S\},
\qquad
\mathfrak a=\operatorname{span}\{D,J\},
\]
where
\[
I=
\begin{pmatrix}
1&0\\0&1
\end{pmatrix},
\quad
S=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&1\\1&0
\end{pmatrix},
\quad
D=
\begin{pmatrix}
1&0\\0&-1
\end{pmatrix},
\quad
J=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&-1\\1&0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\begin{definition}[Fixed-sector quintic and septic coefficients]
\label{def:fixed-sector-quintic-septic}
Let
\[
F_h(z)=A_3(h)z^3+A_5(h)z^5+A_7(h)z^7+\cdots
\]
denote the normalized one-pair quotient odd germ on the cubic-generic region.
Its fixed-sector quintic and septic coefficients are defined by
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h):=\pi_{\mathfrak f}(A_5(h)),
\qquad
A_7^{\mathfrak f}(h):=\pi_{\mathfrak f}(A_7(h)).
\]
Write
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_5(h)\,I+v_5(h)\,S,
\qquad
A_7^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_7(h)\,I+v_7(h)\,S.
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Second residual determinant]
\label{def:second-residual-determinant}
On the cubic-generic region, define
\[
\Delta_7(h):=u_7(h)v_5(h)-u_5(h)v_7(h).
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}[Interpretation]
\label{rem:fixed-sector-residual-package}
The quintic fixed-sector vector \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h)\) refines the first
residual data beyond the trace detector alone, and the determinant
\(\Delta_7(h)\) records the next residual quotient at septic order. In the
working notes, these form the first two nontrivial layers of a one-pair
residual package:
\[
c(h),\qquad A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h),\qquad \Delta_7(h).
\]
The current draft has not yet fully translated these objects into the existing
coefficient notation \((u,v,w,\alpha,\beta,\gamma,r,\dots)\), but they are the
natural variables for the geometric reformulation of the multi-pair problem.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Work in progress: fixed-sector geometry]
\label{rem:wip-fixed-sector-geometry}
The later local analysis suggests the normalized affine chart
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}
\]
on the cubic-generic region. The intended geometric program is to study the
planar curve
\[
h\longmapsto (x(h),y(h))
\]
rather than only the scalar quintic trace. In this formulation, the remaining
generic obstruction is reduced to common four-secant lines of this planar
curve.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Derived structural identities]
\label{rem:derived-fixed-sector-identities}
The working analysis gives the following identities, which should be proved in
a later draft in the paper's native coefficient language.

\begin{enumerate}
\item If
\[
X_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
u&v\\v&w
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_3=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha&\beta\\\beta&\gamma
\end{pmatrix},
\]
then the quintic fixed-sector \(S\)-component coming from the commutator-type
channel is
\[
v_5=\gamma u-\alpha w.
\]

\item The direct linear \(q^{(7)}\)-driven contribution to the septic
fixed-sector coefficient is \(I\)-directional. In particular, on that slice,
\[
v_7^{(q^{(7)})}=0,
\qquad
\Delta_7^{(q^{(7)})}=u_7^{(q^{(7)})}v_5.
\]
Thus the determinant \(\Delta_7\) is the natural second residual scalar once
\(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\) has been fixed.
\end{enumerate}
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Strengthened organizing principle]

\subsection{Normalization gauge at the first residual step}

The phrase ``value/gauge family'' bundles together two logically distinct
sources of ambiguity:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the \emph{pure value family}, recorded coefficientwise by the spaces
\(
V_{\val}^{(2j+1)}\subset \mathcal J_{2j+1},
\)
and

\item the \emph{normalization gauge}, consisting of even scalar
renormalizations and even jet-basis changes.
\end{enumerate}

For the first residual step it is useful to separate these two.

\begin{definition}[Normalization gauge]
\label{def:normalization-gauge}
Let \(F(z)\) be a matrix-valued odd germ. The normalization gauge acts by
\[
F(z)\longmapsto a(z)\,P(z)\,F(z)\,P(z)^{-1},
\]
where
\[
a(z)=1+c_2 z^2+c_4 z^4+\cdots
\]
is an even scalar germ with \(a(0)=1\), and
\[
P(z)=I+B_2 z^2+B_4 z^4+\cdots
\]
is an even \(2\times 2\) matrix germ with \(P(0)=I\).
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[First-residual transformation law]
\label{prop:first-residual-gauge-law}
Let
\[
F(z)=A_3 z^3+A_5 z^5+O(z^7)
\]
be an odd matrix germ, and let
\[
F^{\new}(z):=a(z)\,P(z)\,F(z)\,P(z)^{-1}
\]
with \(a,P\) as in Definition~\ref{def:normalization-gauge}. Then
\[
A_3^{\new}=A_3,
\qquad
A_5^{\new}=A_5+c_2A_3+[B_2,A_3].
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Expand
\[
P(z)^{-1}=I-B_2 z^2+O(z^4).
\]
Then
\[
P(z)F(z)P(z)^{-1}
=
(I+B_2 z^2)\,(A_3 z^3+A_5 z^5)\,(I-B_2 z^2)+O(z^7),
\]
so
\[
P(z)F(z)P(z)^{-1}
=
A_3 z^3+\bigl(A_5+[B_2,A_3]\bigr)z^5+O(z^7).
\]
Multiplying by
\[
a(z)=1+c_2 z^2+O(z^4)
\]
gives
\[
F^{\new}(z)
=
A_3 z^3+\bigl(A_5+c_2A_3+[B_2,A_3]\bigr)z^5+O(z^7),
\]
as claimed.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Residual effect of normalization gauge]
\label{cor:first-residual-gauge-law}
Let \(\ell:=\mathbf C A_3\). Under the normalization gauge,
the quintic coefficient changes by
\[
A_5 \longmapsto A_5 + c_2 A_3 + [B_2,A_3].
\]
Hence its class in the residual quotient modulo the leading line satisfies
\[
[A_5] \longmapsto [A_5+[B_2,A_3}]
\qquad\text{in}\qquad
\mathcal J_5/\ell.
\]
In particular, scalar renormalization is invisible in the first residual
quotient.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
This is immediate from Proposition~\ref{prop:first-residual-gauge-law}, since
\(c_2A_3\in \ell\).
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Trace invariance under normalization gauge]
\label{cor:trace-invariant-under-normalization-gauge}
If \(A_3\) is diagonal-traceless, then every commutator \([B_2,A_3]\) is
traceless. Consequently
\[
\operatorname{tr}(A_5^{\new})=\operatorname{tr}(A_5)
\]
for every normalization-gauge transform \(F^{\new}\) of \(F\).
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
The scalar renormalization term \(c_2A_3\) is traceless because \(A_3\) is.
The commutator term is traceless because
\[
\operatorname{tr}[B_2,A_3]=0.
\]
Hence Proposition~\ref{prop:first-residual-gauge-law} gives
\[
\operatorname{tr}(A_5^{\new})=\operatorname{tr}(A_5).
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[What remains at quintic order]
\label{rem:quintic-pure-value-bottleneck}
The normalization gauge is therefore harmless for the first residual class.
The only remaining obstruction to using trace as a residual detector is the
pure value quintic slice \(V_{\val}^{(5)}\). To make the residual-trace
argument fully rigorous, it suffices to prove
\[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad (h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}),
\]
or more weakly that trace annihilates the image of \(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\) in
the residual quotient.
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Generic first residual nonvanishing under trace-free quintic value slice]
\label{prop:first-residual-class-generic}
Assume that for every \(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\),
\[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\bigr)=0.
\]
Then there exists an open dense subset
\[
\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\subset \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}
\]
such that
\[
\overline v_1(h)\neq 0
\qquad\text{in }Q/\ell_h
\]
for every \(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\).

Equivalently, on \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\) the quintic quotient coefficient
is not collinear with the cubic leading line:
\[
v_1(h)\notin \ell_h.
\]
Hence the one-pair quotient odd germ is not projectively constant on
\(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By Proposition~\ref{prop:cubic-leading-order}, for \(h\in\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\)
the cubic quotient coefficient
\[
v_0(h)=[J_3^{\pair}(h)]
\]
is nonzero and defines the leading line \(\ell_h\), and this line is
diagonal-traceless.

By Corollary~\ref{cor:trace-invariant-under-normalization-gauge}, trace is
invariant under the normalization gauge at the first residual step. By the
present hypothesis, trace also annihilates the pure value quintic slice
\(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\). Therefore trace descends to a well-defined linear
functional
\[
\tau_h:Q/\ell_h\to \mathbf C.
\]

By Lemma~\ref{lem:no-cancellation-quintic-trace},
\[
\tau_h(\overline v_1(h))
=
\operatorname{tr}J_5^{\pair}(h)
\not\equiv 0
\]
as a function of \(h\) on \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\). Since the family is
real-analytic in \(h\), the zero set of
\[
h\longmapsto \tau_h(\overline v_1(h))
\]
is a proper analytic subset of \(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}\). Its complement
\(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\) is therefore open and dense.

For \(h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\) we have
\[
\tau_h(\overline v_1(h))\neq 0,
\]
hence
\[
\overline v_1(h)\neq 0
\qquad\text{in }Q/\ell_h.
\]
Thus the one-pair quotient odd germ is not projectively constant on
\(\mathcal U_{\mathrm{res}}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Work in progress: trace-free pure value quintic slice]
\label{rem:wip-trace-free-pure-value-quintic}
The remaining local task is to prove
\[
\operatorname{tr}\bigl(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\bigr)=0
\qquad (h\in \mathcal U_{\mathrm{cub}}),
\]
or more weakly that trace annihilates the image of \(V_{\val}^{(5)}(h)\) in
the residual quotient. Once this is established,
Proposition~\ref{prop:first-residual-class-generic}
gives a fully rigorous open-dense nonvanishing theorem for the first residual
class.
\end{remark}

\subsection{Fixed-sector refinement at quintic and septic order}

The trace witness at quintic order suggests a more structured refinement of the
one-pair residual germ than the trace alone records. For bookkeeping purposes,
it is convenient to decompose the quintic and septic coefficients into fixed and
anti-fixed parts with respect to the involution
\[
\iota(A):=J_0AJ_0,
\qquad
J_0=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&1\\
1&0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Write
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(A):=\frac12(A+J_0AJ_0),
\qquad
\pi_{\mathfrak a}(A):=\frac12(A-J_0AJ_0).
\]
Then
\[
\mathfrak f=\operatorname{span}\{I,S\},
\qquad
\mathfrak a=\operatorname{span}\{D,J\},
\]
where
\[
I=
\begin{pmatrix}
1&0\\0&1
\end{pmatrix},
\quad
S=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&1\\1&0
\end{pmatrix},
\quad
D=
\begin{pmatrix}
1&0\\0&-1
\end{pmatrix},
\quad
J=
\begin{pmatrix}
0&-1\\1&0
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

\begin{definition}[Fixed-sector quintic and septic coefficients]
\label{def:fixed-sector-quintic-septic}
Let
\[
F_h(z)=A_3(h)z^3+A_5(h)z^5+A_7(h)z^7+\cdots
\]
denote the normalized one-pair quotient odd germ on the cubic-generic region.
Its fixed-sector quintic and septic coefficients are defined by
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h):=\pi_{\mathfrak f}(A_5(h)),
\qquad
A_7^{\mathfrak f}(h):=\pi_{\mathfrak f}(A_7(h)).
\]
Write
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_5(h)\,I+v_5(h)\,S,
\qquad
A_7^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_7(h)\,I+v_7(h)\,S.
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{definition}[Second residual determinant]
\label{def:second-residual-determinant}
On the cubic-generic region, define
\[
\Delta_7(h):=u_7(h)v_5(h)-u_5(h)v_7(h).
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{remark}[Derived fixed-sector identities]
\label{rem:derived-fixed-sector-identities}
The working analysis gives the following identities, which should be proved in
a later draft in the paper's native coefficient language.

\begin{enumerate}
\item If
\[
X_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
u&v\\v&w
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_3=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha&\beta\\\beta&\gamma
\end{pmatrix},
\]
then the quintic fixed-sector \(S\)-component coming from the commutator-type
channel is
\[
v_5=\gamma u-\alpha w.
\]

\item The direct linear \(q^{(7)}\)-driven contribution to the septic
fixed-sector coefficient is \(I\)-directional. In particular, on that slice,
\[
v_7^{(q^{(7)})}=0,
\qquad
\Delta_7^{(q^{(7)})}=u_7^{(q^{(7)})}v_5.
\]
Thus the determinant \(\Delta_7\) is the natural second residual scalar once
\(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\) has been fixed.
\end{enumerate}
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Work in progress: normalized affine one-pair invariant curve]
\label{rem:wip-normalized-affine-one-pair-curve}
The later working analysis suggests the normalized affine chart
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}
\]
on the cubic-generic region. The intended geometric program is to study the
planar curve
\[
h\longmapsto (x(h),y(h))
\]
rather than only the scalar quintic trace. In this formulation, the remaining
generic obstruction is reduced to common four-secant lines of this planar
curve.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Work in progress: planar convexity reformulation]
\label{rem:wip-planar-convexity-reformulation}
The later working analysis suggests that the remaining generic obstruction
should be reformulated as a planar geometry problem for the curve
\[
h\longmapsto \left(\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}\right).
\]
On the open set where
\[
c(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
\frac{d}{dh}\!\left(\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}\right)\neq 0,
\qquad
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
x'(h) & y'(h)\\
x''(h) & y''(h)
\end{pmatrix}\neq 0,
\]
with
\[
x(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h)=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)},
\]
the image breaks into strictly convex graph components. On each such component,
a line meets the curve in at most two points. The remaining generic global
obstruction is therefore reduced to the possibility of a common four-secant
line meeting two separated convex components twice each.

In this formulation, the unresolved endgame is no longer local quintic
bookkeeping but endpoint/support-function geometry for the planar curve
\((v_5/c,\Delta_7/c)\).
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Explicit fixed-sector formulas at quintic order]
\label{prop:explicit-quintic-fixed-sector}
Write
\[
X_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
u&v\\
v&w
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_3=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha&\beta\\
\beta&\gamma
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the fixed-sector part of the basic quintic commutator channel
\[
X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3
\]
is
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3)
=
\bigl(v(\gamma-\alpha)+\beta(u-w)\bigr)I
+
(\gamma u-\alpha w)S.
\]
Consequently, for the quintic fixed-sector coefficient
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_5(h)\,I+v_5(h)\,S,
\]
the commutator-type contribution is explicitly
\[
u_5^{(\mathrm{comm})}
=
v(\gamma-\alpha)+\beta(u-w),
\qquad
v_5^{(\mathrm{comm})}
=
\gamma u-\alpha w.
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
A direct multiplication gives
\[
X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-2\alpha\beta+2\beta u & \gamma u-\alpha w\\[.5ex]
\gamma u-\alpha w & -2\beta w+2\gamma\beta
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and hence its fixed-sector projection is obtained by taking the average of the
diagonal entries for the \(I\)-component and the common off-diagonal entry for
the \(S\)-component:
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3)
=
\frac{(-2\alpha v+2\beta u)+(-2\beta w+2\gamma v)}{2}\,I
+
(\gamma u-\alpha w)S.
\]
This simplifies to
\[
\bigl(v(\gamma-\alpha)+\beta(u-w)\bigr)I
+
(\gamma u-\alpha w)S,
\]
as claimed.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Explicit septic \(K_1\)-transport fixed-sector formulas]
\label{prop:explicit-septic-k1-fixed-sector}
Write
\[
X_0=\diag(x_1,x_2),
\qquad
X_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
u&v\\
v&w
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_2=
\begin{pmatrix}
a&b\\
b&c
\end{pmatrix},
\]
\[
X_4=
\begin{pmatrix}
p&r\\
r&s
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_5=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha_5&\beta_5\\
\beta_5&\gamma_5
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_6=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mu_6&\nu_6\\
\nu_6&\lambda_6
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the fixed-sector parts of the three septic \(K_1\)-transport channels are
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_5JX_1-X_1JX_5)
=
\bigl(\beta_5(u-w)+v(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)\bigr)I
+
(\gamma_5u-\alpha_5w)S,
\]
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_2JX_4-X_4JX_2)
=
\bigl(b(p-s)+r(c-a)\bigr)I
+
(cp-as)S,
\]
and
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_0JX_6-X_6JX_0)
=
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6\,I
+
(x_2\mu_6-x_1\lambda_6)S.
\]

Consequently, if the septic \(K_1\)-transport fixed-sector coefficient is
written
\[
A_{7,K_1}^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_{7,K_1}(h)\,I+v_{7,K_1}(h)\,S,
\]
then
\[
u_{7,K_1}
=
\kappa\Bigl(
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6
+
\beta_5(u-w)+v(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)
+
b(p-s)+r(c-a)
\Bigr),
\]
\[
v_{7,K_1}
=
\kappa\Bigl(
x_2\mu_6-x_1\lambda_6
+
\gamma_5u-\alpha_5w
+
cp-as
\Bigr).
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
For two symmetric matrices
\[
X=
\begin{pmatrix}
a&b\\
b&c
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
Y=
\begin{pmatrix}
p&q\\
q&r
\end{pmatrix},
\]
a direct calculation gives
\[
XJY-YJX
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-2aq+2bp & -ar+cp\\
-ar+cp & -2br+2cq
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Taking fixed-sector projection yields
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(XJY-YJX)
=
\bigl(b(p-r)+q(c-a)\bigr)I
+
(cp-ar)S.
\]
Applying this formula to \((X,Y)=(X_5,X_1)\) and \((X,Y)=(X_2,X_4)\) gives the
first two displayed identities.

For the \(X_0/X_6\) channel, a direct multiplication gives
\[
X_0JX_6-X_6JX_0
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-2x_1\nu_6 & -x_1\lambda_6+x_2\mu_6\\
-x_1\lambda_6+x_2\mu_6 & 2x_2\nu_6
\end{pmatrix},
\]
whose fixed-sector part is
\[
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6\,I
+
(x_2\mu_6-x_1\lambda_6)S.
\]
Summing the three channels gives the stated formulas for
\(u_{7,K_1}\) and \(v_{7,K_1}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven septic slice]
\label{cor:direct-q7-septic-slice}
On the direct linear \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice, the septic \(K_1\)-transport
fixed-sector contribution is \(I\)-directional. More precisely,
\[
v_{7,K_1}^{(q^{(7)})}=0,
\]
and
\[
u_{7,K_1}^{(q^{(7)})}
=
\kappa\Bigl(
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6^{(q^{(7)})}
+
v\,(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)^{(q^{(7)})}
\Bigr),
\]
so in particular
\[
\Delta_7^{(q^{(7)})}=u_7^{(q^{(7)})}v_5.
\]
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
On the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice, the linear \(X_5\)-input is diagonal,
hence
\[
\beta_5^{(q^{(7)})}=0.
\]
The mixed \(X_6\)-slice is off-diagonal on this grading, so
\[
\mu_6^{(q^{(7)})}=\lambda_6^{(q^{(7)})}=0.
\]
Moreover the \(X_2/X_4\)-channel does not lie on the pure
\((K_1,\eta_2,q^{(7)})\)-slice. Therefore the \(S\)-component vanishes and the
displayed formula for \(u_{7,K_1}^{(q^{(7)})}\) follows from
Proposition~\ref{prop:explicit-septic-k1-fixed-sector}. Since
\[
\Delta_7=u_7v_5-u_5v_7,
\]
the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice satisfies
\[
\Delta_7^{(q^{(7)})}=u_7^{(q^{(7)})}v_5.
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Status of explicit fixed-sector formulas]
\label{rem:status-explicit-fixed-sector-formulas}
Proposition~\ref{prop:explicit-quintic-fixed-sector} gives an explicit
coefficient formula for the quintic commutator contribution to
\(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), and
Proposition~\ref{prop:explicit-septic-k1-fixed-sector} gives an explicit
formula for the septic \(K_1\)-transport contribution to
\(A_7^{\mathfrak f}\). What is still not known in full explicit form is the
total septic fixed-sector coefficient after all order-7 sectors are included.
Thus the formulas above should be read as the explicit transport-level
contribution underlying the second residual determinant \(\Delta_7\), not yet
as a closed formula for the full total pair \((u_7,v_7)\).
\end{remark}

\begin{proposition}[Explicit fixed-sector formulas at quintic order]
\label{prop:explicit-quintic-fixed-sector}
Write
\[
X_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
u&v\\
v&w
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_3=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha&\beta\\
\beta&\gamma
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the fixed-sector part of the basic quintic commutator channel
\[
X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3
\]
is
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3)
=
\bigl(v(\gamma-\alpha)+\beta(u-w)\bigr)I
+
(\gamma u-\alpha w)S.
\]
Consequently, for the quintic fixed-sector coefficient
\[
A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_5(h)\,I+v_5(h)\,S,
\]
the commutator-type contribution is explicitly
\[
u_5^{(\mathrm{comm})}
=
v(\gamma-\alpha)+\beta(u-w),
\qquad
v_5^{(\mathrm{comm})}
=
\gamma u-\alpha w.
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
A direct computation gives
\[
X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-2\alpha v+2\beta u & -\alpha w+\gamma u\\[.5ex]
-\alpha w+\gamma u & -2\beta w+2\gamma v
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
The fixed-sector projection of a symmetric matrix
\[
\begin{pmatrix}
A&B\\
B&C
\end{pmatrix}
\]
is
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}
\begin{pmatrix}
A&B\\
B&C
\end{pmatrix}
=
\frac{A+C}{2}\,I+B\,S.
\]
Applying this to the displayed matrix gives
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_3JX_1-X_1JX_3)
=
\frac{(-2\alpha v+2\beta u)+(-2\beta w+2\gamma v)}{2}\,I
+
(\gamma u-\alpha w)S,
\]
which simplifies to
\[
\bigl(v(\gamma-\alpha)+\beta(u-w)\bigr)I
+
(\gamma u-\alpha w)S.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Explicit septic \(K_1\)-transport fixed-sector formulas]
\label{prop:explicit-septic-k1-fixed-sector}
Write
\[
X_0=\diag(x_1,x_2),
\qquad
X_1=
\begin{pmatrix}
u&v\\
v&w
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_2=
\begin{pmatrix}
a&b\\
b&c
\end{pmatrix},
\]
\[
X_4=
\begin{pmatrix}
p&r\\
r&s
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_5=
\begin{pmatrix}
\alpha_5&\beta_5\\
\beta_5&\gamma_5
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
X_6=
\begin{pmatrix}
\mu_6&\nu_6\\
\nu_6&\lambda_6
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Then the fixed-sector parts of the three septic \(K_1\)-transport channels are
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_5JX_1-X_1JX_5)
=
\bigl(\beta_5(u-w)+v(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)\bigr)I
+
(\gamma_5u-\alpha_5w)S,
\]
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_2JX_4-X_4JX_2)
=
\bigl(b(p-s)+r(c-a)\bigr)I
+
(cp-as)S,
\]
and
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(X_0JX_6-X_6JX_0)
=
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6\,I
+
(x_2\mu_6-x_1\lambda_6)S.
\]

Consequently, if the septic \(K_1\)-transport fixed-sector coefficient is
written
\[
A_{7,K_1}^{\mathfrak f}(h)=u_{7,K_1}(h)\,I+v_{7,K_1}(h)\,S,
\]
then
\[
u_{7,K_1}
=
\kappa\Bigl(
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6
+
\beta_5(u-w)+v(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)
+
b(p-s)+r(c-a)
\Bigr),
\]
\[
v_{7,K_1}
=
\kappa\Bigl(
x_2\mu_6-x_1\lambda_6
+
\gamma_5u-\alpha_5w
+
cp-as
\Bigr).
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
For symmetric matrices
\[
X=
\begin{pmatrix}
a&b\\
b&c
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
Y=
\begin{pmatrix}
p&q\\
q&r
\end{pmatrix},
\]
a direct computation gives
\[
XJY-YJX
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-2aq+2bp & -ar+cp\\
-ar+cp & -2br+2cq
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Projecting to the fixed sector yields
\[
\pi_{\mathfrak f}(XJY-YJX)
=
\bigl(b(p-r)+q(c-a)\bigr)I
+
(cp-ar)S.
\]
Applying this formula to \((X,Y)=(X_5,X_1)\) and \((X,Y)=(X_2,X_4)\) gives the
first two displayed identities.

For the \(X_0/X_6\) channel, one computes directly
\[
X_0JX_6-X_6JX_0
=
\begin{pmatrix}
-2x_1\nu_6 & -x_1\lambda_6+x_2\mu_6\\
-x_1\lambda_6+x_2\mu_6 & 2x_2\nu_6
\end{pmatrix},
\]
whose fixed-sector projection is
\[
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6\,I
+
(x_2\mu_6-x_1\lambda_6)S.
\]
Summing the three fixed-sector contributions gives the stated formulas for
\(u_{7,K_1}\) and \(v_{7,K_1}\).
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven septic slice]
\label{cor:direct-q7-septic-slice}
On the direct linear \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice, the septic \(K_1\)-transport
fixed-sector contribution is \(I\)-directional. More precisely,
\[
v_{7,K_1}^{(q^{(7)})}=0,
\]
and
\[
u_{7,K_1}^{(q^{(7)})}
=
\kappa\Bigl(
(x_2-x_1)\nu_6^{(q^{(7)})}
+
v\,(\gamma_5-\alpha_5)^{(q^{(7)})}
\Bigr).
\]
In particular,
\[
\Delta_7^{(q^{(7)})}=u_7^{(q^{(7)})}v_5.
\]
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
On the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice, the linear \(X_5\)-input is diagonal,
so
\[
\beta_5^{(q^{(7)})}=0.
\]
The mixed \(X_6\)-slice is off-diagonal on this grading, so
\[
\mu_6^{(q^{(7)})}=\lambda_6^{(q^{(7)})}=0.
\]
Moreover the \(X_2/X_4\)-channel does not lie on the pure
\((K_1,\eta_2,q^{(7)})\)-slice. Therefore the \(S\)-component vanishes and the
displayed formula for \(u_{7,K_1}^{(q^{(7)})}\) follows from
Proposition~\ref{prop:explicit-septic-k1-fixed-sector}. Since
\[
\Delta_7=u_7v_5-u_5v_7,
\]
the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice satisfies
\[
\Delta_7^{(q^{(7)})}=u_7^{(q^{(7)})}v_5.
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Status of explicit fixed-sector formulas]
\label{rem:status-explicit-fixed-sector-formulas}
Proposition~\ref{prop:explicit-quintic-fixed-sector} gives an explicit
coefficient formula for the quintic commutator contribution to
\(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), and
Proposition~\ref{prop:explicit-septic-k1-fixed-sector} gives an explicit
formula for the septic \(K_1\)-transport contribution to
\(A_7^{\mathfrak f}\). What is still not known in full explicit form is the
total septic fixed-sector coefficient after all order-\(7\) sectors are
included. Thus the formulas above should be read as the explicit
transport-level contribution underlying the second residual determinant
\(\Delta_7\), not yet as a closed formula for the full total pair
\((u_7,v_7)\).
\end{remark}

\subsection{Normalized planar curve and curvature bridge}

On the region where \(c(h)\neq 0\), define the normalized scalars
\[
\rho_1(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
\rho_2(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)},
\]
and the associated planar invariant curve
\[
\Gamma(h):=\bigl(\rho_1(h),\rho_2(h)\bigr).
\]

\begin{proposition}[Non-affinity of the second normalized residual scalar]
\label{prop:rho2-not-affine-in-rho1}
Assume that the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice of \(\Delta_7(h)\) is
generically nonzero on the quintic-generic region. Then there do not exist
constants \(A,B\in\mathbf C\) such that
\[
\rho_2(h)\equiv A\,\rho_1(h)+B
\]
on any connected component of \(\{h:c(h)\neq 0\}\).

Equivalently,
\[
\Delta_7(h)\not\equiv A\,v_5(h)+B\,c(h).
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Suppose
\[
\Delta_7(h)\equiv A\,v_5(h)+B\,c(h)
\]
on a connected component of \(\{c\neq 0\}\). Then the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven
slice of the right-hand side vanishes, because \(v_5\) and \(c\) are governed
by the cubic/quintic layers and carry no direct septic \(q^{(7)}\)-slice.
But the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice of \(\Delta_7\) is generically nonzero
by hypothesis. This is a contradiction. Dividing by \(c(h)\) yields
\[
\rho_2(h)\not\equiv A\,\rho_1(h)+B.
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Normalized derivative numerators]
\label{def:MN-normalized-curve}
Define
\[
M(h):=c(h)v_5'(h)-c'(h)v_5(h),
\qquad
N(h):=c(h)\Delta_7'(h)-c'(h)\Delta_7(h).
\]
Then
\[
\rho_1'(h)=\frac{M(h)}{c(h)^2},
\qquad
\rho_2'(h)=\frac{N(h)}{c(h)^2}.
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Curvature bridge for the normalized planar curve]
\label{prop:curvature-bridge-normalized-curve}
Let
\[
K_\Gamma(h):=\rho_1'(h)\rho_2''(h)-\rho_2'(h)\rho_1''(h)
\]
denote the affine curvature numerator of the planar curve \(\Gamma(h)\).
Then
\[
K_\Gamma(h)=\frac{M(h)N'(h)-N(h)M'(h)}{c(h)^4}.
\]
In particular, if \(K_\Gamma\equiv 0\) on a connected open set on which
\(M\neq 0\), then \(\rho_2\) is affine in \(\rho_1\) on that set.
Consequently, under the hypothesis of
Proposition~\ref{prop:rho2-not-affine-in-rho1}, one has
\[
K_\Gamma\not\equiv 0
\]
on the generic region.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
By Definition~\ref{def:MN-normalized-curve},
\[
\rho_1'=\frac{M}{c^2},
\qquad
\rho_2'=\frac{N}{c^2}.
\]
Differentiating gives
\[
\rho_1''=\frac{M'c-2Mc'}{c^3},
\qquad
\rho_2''=\frac{N'c-2Nc'}{c^3}.
\]
Therefore
\[
K_\Gamma
=
\frac{M}{c^2}\frac{N'c-2Nc'}{c^3}
-
\frac{N}{c^2}\frac{M'c-2Mc'}{c^3}
=
\frac{MN'-NM'}{c^4}.
\]

If \(K_\Gamma\equiv 0\) on a connected open set with \(M\neq 0\), then
\[
0=MN'-NM'=M^2\left(\frac{N}{M}\right)',
\]
so \(N/M\) is constant there:
\[
\frac{N}{M}=A.
\]
Since
\[
\frac{N}{M}=\frac{\rho_2'}{\rho_1'},
\]
it follows that
\[
\rho_2'=A\,\rho_1',
\]
hence
\[
\rho_2=A\,\rho_1+B
\]
on that connected set. The final claim now follows from
Proposition~\ref{prop:rho2-not-affine-in-rho1}.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Geometric consequence]
\label{rem:geometric-consequence-normalized-curve}
Proposition~\ref{prop:curvature-bridge-normalized-curve} is the local-to-global
bridge from the order-\(7\) fixed-sector analysis back to the secant/tangent
program. On the open set where
\[
c(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
M(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
M(h)N'(h)-N(h)M'(h)\neq 0,
\]
the normalized planar curve \(\Gamma(h)\) is a strictly convex graph in the
affine chart \((\rho_1,\rho_2)\). Thus the remaining global obstruction is a
common four-secant line meeting two separated convex components twice each.
\end{remark}

Two small notes.

First, this is written in the same honest style as the rest of the current draft: the non-affinity proposition is conditional on the direct septic witness being generically nonzero. That is the cleanest place to plug in the local order-7 witness once you decide how hard you want to state it.

Second, once this block is in, the later global remarks about planar convexity and the ‚Äúcurrent geometric bottleneck‚Äù become much better anchored, because the curve and its curvature are now actually defined in theorem-proof form rather than only in remarks.

4. Why one zero is already unstable

Here is the best structural argument I can see.

T(h‚àó,h):=Œõh‚àó(h).
T(h‚àó‚Äã,h):=Œõh‚àó‚Äã‚Äã(h).

This is smooth off the diagonal and vanishes to second order on the diagonal with sign governed by WW.

Now look at the set
Z:={(h‚àó,h):T(h‚àó,h)=0, h<h‚àó}.
Z:={(h‚àó‚Äã,h):T(h‚àó‚Äã,h)=0, h<h‚àó‚Äã}.

along such a branch would force a compatibility between the tangent direction at h‚àóh‚àó‚Äã and the secant direction from the left interval.

But that is exactly the kind of degeneracy the nonvanishing of the projective Wronskian WW is meant to prevent: W‚âÝ0WÓÄÝ=0 says the projective jet
F, F‚Ä≤, F‚Ä≤‚Ä≤
F, F‚Ä≤, F‚Ä≤‚Ä≤

is nondegenerate, so nearby secants cannot align with the osculating direction except at the basepoint itself.

In plainer language: on an ECT arc, the tangent line should be a genuine support line, and the only way it can pick up an extra adjacent-interval intersection is if the two adjacent arcs line up in a projectively degenerate way across the boundary.

So the natural next theorem is:
Across an adjacent boundary, the one-sided tangent separators inherit the support sign from the nonvanishing of W.
Across an adjacent boundary, the one-sided tangent separators inherit the support sign from the nonvanishing of W.‚Äã

That is not yet proved, but it is now much sharper than before.

\begin{definition}[Projective Wronskian of the strengthened one-pair triple]
\label{def:projective-wronskian-c-v5-delta7}
Define
\[
W(h):=
\det\!
\begin{pmatrix}
c(h) & v_5(h) & \Delta_7(h)\\
c'(h) & v_5'(h) & \Delta_7'(h)\\
c''(h) & v_5''(h) & \Delta_7''(h)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
\end{definition}

\begin{proposition}[Projective-Wronskian bridge]
\label{prop:projective-wronskian-bridge}
With \(M(h)\) and \(N(h)\) as in
Definition~\ref{def:MN-normalized-curve}, one has
\[
M(h)N'(h)-N(h)M'(h)=c(h)\,W(h).
\]
Consequently,
\[
K_\Gamma(h)=\frac{W(h)}{c(h)^3}
\]
on the region where \(c(h)\neq 0\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Using
\[
M=c\,v_5'-c'v_5,
\qquad
N=c\,\Delta_7'-c'\Delta_7,
\]
a direct expansion gives
\[
M N'-N M'
=
(c\,v_5'-c'v_5)(c\,\Delta_7''-c''\Delta_7)
-
(c\,\Delta_7'-c'\Delta_7)(c\,v_5''-c''v_5).
\]
Expanding and regrouping yields
\[
M N'-N M'
=
c\,
\det\!
\begin{pmatrix}
c & v_5 & \Delta_7\\
c' & v_5' & \Delta_7'\\
c'' & v_5'' & \Delta_7''
\end{pmatrix}
=
c\,W.
\]
The displayed formula for \(K_\Gamma\) now follows from
Proposition~\ref{prop:curvature-bridge-normalized-curve}.
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[ECT intervals for the strengthened one-pair triple]
\label{prop:ect-intervals-c-v5-delta7}
Let \(I\) be a connected interval on which
\[
c(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
M(h)\neq 0,
\qquad
W(h)\neq 0.
\]
Then the triple
\[
(c,\ v_5,\ \Delta_7)
\]
is an extended complete Chebyshev system on \(I\). Equivalently, every
nontrivial linear combination
\[
A\,c(h)+B\,v_5(h)+C\,\Delta_7(h)
\]
has at most two zeros on \(I\), counting multiplicity.

In particular, in the affine chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c},
\]
every affine line meets \(\Gamma(I)\) in at most two points, counting
tangency with multiplicity.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
On \(I\), the successive initial Wronskians are
\[
c,\qquad
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}c&v_5\\ c'&v_5'\end{pmatrix}=M,
\qquad
\det\!\begin{pmatrix}
c&v_5&\Delta_7\\
c'&v_5'&\Delta_7'\\
c''&v_5''&\Delta_7''
\end{pmatrix}=W.
\]
Since all three are nonvanishing on \(I\), the standard ECT criterion applies.
The affine-line statement follows by writing
\[
A\,c+B\,v_5+C\,\Delta_7=0
\]
as the equation of a line in the chart \((v_5/c,\Delta_7/c)\).
\end{proof}

\begin{definition}[Tangent-separator kernel]
\label{def:tangent-separator-kernel}
Let
\[
F(h):=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]
For \(s,t\) in the generic region, define
\[
K(s,t):=\det\!\bigl(F(s),F'(s),F(t)\bigr).
\]
For fixed \(s\), the zeros of \(t\mapsto K(s,t)\) are exactly the
intersections of the tangent line at \(\Gamma(s)\) with the curve
\(\Gamma(t)\).
\end{definition}

\begin{lemma}[Basic properties of the tangent-separator kernel]
\label{lem:tangent-separator-basic-properties}
For every \(s\) in the generic region,
\[
K(s,s)=0,
\qquad
\partial_tK(s,s)=0,
\qquad
\partial_t^2K(s,s)=W(s).
\]
In particular, on any interval where \(W\) has fixed sign, the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(s)\) is locally supporting.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
The first two identities follow because, at \(t=s\), the third column of the
determinant equals \(F(s)\), and after differentiating once in \(t\) it equals
\(F'(s)\). Differentiating a second time gives
\[
\partial_t^2K(s,s)=
\det\!\bigl(F(s),F'(s),F''(s)\bigr)=W(s).
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Audit: direct septic witness]
\label{rem:audit-direct-septic-witness}
The use of Proposition~\ref{prop:rho2-not-affine-in-rho1} still depends on the
claim that the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-driven slice of \(\Delta_7\) is generically
nonzero. The current draft contains the explicit \(K_1\)-transport formulas
and the \(I\)-directionality of the direct \(q^{(7)}\)-slice, but the final
generic noncancellation statement for that slice should still be audited as a
standalone local theorem.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Audit: ECT criterion usage]
\label{rem:audit-ect-criterion-usage}
Proposition~\ref{prop:ect-intervals-c-v5-delta7} uses the standard ECT
criterion for triples of \(C^2\) functions with nonvanishing successive initial
Wronskians. The current draft should retain an explicit audit note that the
regularity and sign hypotheses needed for this criterion are satisfied on the
intended generic intervals.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Audit: tangent-separator sign regularity]
\label{rem:audit-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}
The remaining global obstruction is the sign behavior of
\[
K(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t))
\]
across adjacent ECT intervals. What still needs to be audited is the claim that
for adjacent intervals \(I_1<I_2\), one has
\[
K(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad (s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1),
\]
equivalently, that tangent lines from the right interval do not meet the
adjacent left interval.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Audit: global no-four-secant theorem]
\label{rem:audit-global-no-four-secant}
Assuming the adjacent tangent-separator sign theorem, the support intervals of
distinct ECT branches are disjoint, and hence no affine line can be 2-secant to
two different branches. The deduction from adjacent tangent separation to a
global no-four-secant theorem should still be audited carefully in the final
endgame writeup.
\end{remark}

1. Does a ‚Äúfirst zero‚Äù exist?

As stated, this is shaky.

You defined
S={‚Äâs‚ààI2:‚àÉt‚ààI1, K(s,t)=0‚Äâ}

    and the ordering parameter on I2I2‚Äã is complete enough that an infimum is realized.

On an open interval I2I2‚Äã, an infimum need not lie in I2I2‚Äã. So the raw ‚Äútake the first zero‚Äù move is not automatically valid.
Fix

J2‚ãêI2
J2‚Äã‚ãêI2‚Äã

whose left endpoint is still to the right of the boundary-near region where you already know K‚âÝ0KÓÄÝ=0. Do the same for
J1‚ãêI1.

Then if there is any zero at all, there is a first zero in the xx-ordering on the compact set J2√óJ1J2‚Äã√óJ1‚Äã, because the zero set of a continuous function is closed.

So the first-zero argument should be phrased on compactly truncated adjacent intervals, not on the raw open intervals.

That fixes the existence issue.
