\subsection{From the one-pair residual germ to two-pair and minimal-core rigidity}

Insert this exact LaTeX

\begin{proposition}[Conditional adjacent tangent separation implies global no-secant]
\label{prop:conditional-global-no-secant}
Assume the following adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity statement:

for every adjacent pair of ECT intervals \(I_1<I_2\) in the affine
\(x=v_5/c\) ordering, one has
\[
K(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1),
\]
where
\[
K(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]

Then no affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals. Equivalently, there is no
global four-secant line for the normalized planar curve \(\Gamma(h)\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals, and let \(L\) be any tangent line to
the branch \(\Gamma(I_2)\). Then \(L\) is the tangent line at some point
\(\Gamma(s)\) with \(s\in I_2\). By the adjacent tangent-separator hypothesis,
the function
\[
t\longmapsto K(s,t)
\]
has no zero on \(I_1\). Hence \(L\) does not meet \(\Gamma(I_1)\), so
\(\Gamma(I_1)\) lies strictly on one side of every support/tangent line of
\(\Gamma(I_2)\).

In support-function language, for every slope \(m\) realized on \(I_2\), the
upper support level of \(I_1\) does not exceed the lower support level of
\(I_2\). Thus the intercept intervals of \(I_1\) and \(I_2\) are disjoint for
every common slope.

By transitivity over the \(x\)-ordering of adjacent intervals, the support
intervals of any two distinct ECT intervals are disjoint. Therefore no affine
line can be \(2\)-secant to two different ECT intervals. In particular, no
global four-secant line exists.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Conditional exclusion of pair-pair branch configurations]
\label{cor:conditional-pair-pair-exclusion}
Under the hypothesis of
Proposition~\ref{prop:conditional-global-no-secant}, a bad finite core cannot
contain two distinct ECT intervals each contributing a pair of points.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Each ECT interval contributes at most two points to the zero set of any affine
line in the normalized chart. By
Proposition~\ref{prop:conditional-global-no-secant}, no affine line can be
\(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals. Hence a pair-pair configuration
across two distinct ECT intervals is impossible.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Audit: first-zero compactness repair]
\label{rem:audit-first-zero-compactness}
Any eventual proof of adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity should avoid
the informal phrase ‚Äútake the first zero on the adjacent interval pair‚Äù on raw
open intervals. The correct setup is to remove a boundary-near region on which
\(K(s,t)\) is already known to have fixed sign, truncate to compact subintervals
\(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\), and then work with the closed zero set
of
\[
K(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t))
\]
inside \(J_2\times J_1\). This is the right framework in which a minimal/first
zero can be realized and the implicit-function-theorem argument can be applied
rigorously.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Audit: adjacent slope-range hypotheses]
\label{rem:audit-adjacent-slope-range-hypotheses}
The current adjacent-interval attack uses disjointness of the interior tangent
slope ranges in the two generic boundary cases:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(W=0\), \(M\neq 0\), where one must use continuity of
\[
m(h)=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
across the boundary together with the sign change of
\[
m'(h)=\frac{W(h)}{M(h)^2}
\]
to conclude that the two one-sided slope intervals meet only at the boundary
endpoint value;

\item \(M=0\), \(M'\neq 0\), where one must also assume
\[
N(h_*)\neq 0
\]
at the turning point \(h_*\) in order to obtain the one-sided blow-up
\[
m(h)\to \pm\infty
\]
and hence disjoint slope ranges.
\end{enumerate}

These hypotheses should be stated explicitly in any final proof of the adjacent
tangent-separator theorem.
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Audit: first-zero implies tangency]
\label{rem:audit-first-zero-implies-tangency}
In the current adjacent-interval strategy, the key differential step is:
if \((s_*,t_*)\) is a first zero of \(K(s,t)\) in the \(s\)-ordering, then that
zero cannot be transverse in \(t\). Equivalently,
\[
K(s_*,t_*)=0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
\partial_t K(s_*,t_*)=0
\]
at the first appearance of a zero. This is what upgrades a first adjacent
intersection into a common tangent line. The final proof should isolate this
step explicitly as an application of the implicit function theorem on the
compactly truncated adjacent interval pair.
\end{remark}

10. audit-global-no-four-secant

    tangent lines from the right interval miss the adjacent left interval,

    therefore support intervals of adjacent branches are disjoint,

    therefore support intervals of any distinct branches are disjoint,

    therefore no line is 2-secant to two different ECT branches,

    therefore no global 4-secant exists.

A. Insert two lemmas that close the ‚Äúfirst zero‚Äù audits
Where to insert

Insert these immediately after

Insert this exact LaTeX

\begin{lemma}[Compact first-zero reduction on adjacent intervals]
\label{lem:compact-first-zero-reduction}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals in the affine
\(x=v_5/c\) ordering. Assume there are compact subintervals
\[
J_1\Subset I_1,\qquad J_2\Subset I_2
\]
such that every zero of the tangent-separator kernel
\[
K(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)),
\]
with \(s\in I_2\), \(t\in I_1\), lies in \(J_2\times J_1\).

If \(K\) has a zero on \(J_2\times J_1\), then there exists a zero
\[
(s_*,t_*)\in J_2\times J_1
\]
such that \(s_*\) is minimal in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all
\(s\)-coordinates of zeros of \(K\) on \(J_2\times J_1\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Because \(K\) is continuous, its zero set in the compact set \(J_2\times J_1\)
is closed, hence compact. The projection of that zero set to the \(s\)-axis is
therefore compact as well. Since \(x=v_5/c\) is monotone on \(I_2\), the
induced \(x\)-ordering is equivalent to the interval ordering on \(J_2\). Thus
the projected zero set has a minimal element \(s_*\), and there exists
\(t_*\in J_1\) such that \((s_*,t_*)\) is a zero of \(K\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[First adjacent zero implies tangency]
\label{lem:first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency}
In the setting of Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-zero-reduction}, let
\((s_*,t_*)\) be a zero with minimal \(s\)-coordinate in the
\(x=v_5/c\) ordering. Then
\[
\partial_t K(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
Consequently,
\[
K(s_*,t_*)=0,
\qquad
\partial_t K(s_*,t_*)=0
\]
force the tangent line at \(\Gamma(s_*)\) to be tangent also at
\(\Gamma(t_*)\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
If \(\partial_tK(s_*,t_*)\neq 0\), the implicit function theorem gives a local
\(C^1\) zero curve
\[
t=t(s)
\]
through \((s_*,t_*)\). In particular, for \(s\) sufficiently close to \(s_*\),
there are zeros of \(K(s,t(s))\). This produces zeros with \(s<s_*\),
contradicting the minimality of \(s_*\). Hence \(\partial_tK(s_*,t_*)=0\).

The identities
\[
K(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
\partial_tK(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F'(t))
\]
show that \(K(s_*,t_*)=0\) means \(\Gamma(t_*)\) lies on the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(s_*)\), while \(\partial_tK(s_*,t_*)=0\) means the tangent direction
at \(t_*\) lies on the same line. Thus the line is tangent at both points.
\end{proof}

B. Insert a proposition that closes the ‚Äúadjacent slope-range hypotheses‚Äù audit
Where to insert

Insert this immediately after the two lemmas above.
Insert this exact LaTeX

\begin{proposition}[Adjacent slope-range separation under generic boundary hypotheses]
\label{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals, meeting at a boundary point \(h_b\).
Assume one of the following generic boundary hypotheses holds:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(W(h_b)=0\), \(M(h_b)\neq 0\), the slope
\[
m(h):=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
extends continuously across \(h_b\), and \(W\) changes sign across \(h_b\);

\item \(M(h_b)=0\), \(M'(h_b)\neq 0\), and \(N(h_b)\neq 0\).
\end{enumerate}

Then the interior tangent-slope ranges of \(I_1\) and \(I_2\) are disjoint.
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
In case (1), one has
\[
m'(h)=\frac{W(h)}{M(h)^2}.
\]
Since \(M(h_b)\neq 0\), the slope \(m\) is continuous at \(h_b\). Because \(W\)
changes sign across \(h_b\), the one-sided restrictions of \(m\) are monotone
with opposite monotonicity, and the common boundary value \(m(h_b)\) is an
endpoint extremum on each side. Hence the interior slope ranges on \(I_1\) and
\(I_2\) meet at most at the boundary endpoint value, so their interiors are
disjoint.

In case (2), \(M(h_b)=0\) is a simple zero and \(N(h_b)\neq 0\). Therefore
\[
m(h)=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
has opposite one-sided blow-up:
\[
m(h)\to \pm\infty
\]
as \(h\to h_b^\pm\). Hence the one-sided slope ranges are disjoint.
\end{proof}

This removes the need for audit-adjacent-slope-range-hypotheses.

\begin{proposition}[Conditional global no-secant theorem]
\label{prop:conditional-global-no-secant}
Assume that for every adjacent pair of ECT intervals \(I_1<I_2\), one has
\[
K(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1),
\]
where
\[
K(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]
Then no affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals. In particular, there is no
global four-secant line for the normalized planar curve \(\Gamma(h)\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Fix adjacent ECT intervals \(I_1<I_2\). A support/tangent line to
\(\Gamma(I_2)\) at a point \(\Gamma(s)\), \(s\in I_2\), meets \(\Gamma(I_1)\)
exactly at zeros of \(t\mapsto K(s,t)\). By hypothesis, no such zero exists, so
the support intervals of \(I_1\) and \(I_2\) are disjoint for every common
slope.

By transitivity over the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering, support intervals of any two
distinct ECT intervals are disjoint. Hence no affine line can be \(2\)-secant
to two different ECT intervals, so no global four-secant line exists.
\end{proof}

This lets you remove audit-global-no-four-secant as a separate audit remark, because it is now a formal conditional consequence.

audit-first-zero-compactness and audit-first-zero-implies-tangency are also reasonable to delegate, even though they are now mostly repaired. Another chat can act like a referee and check whether the compact truncation plus implicit-function-theorem argument is airtight.

audit-adjacent-slope-range-hypotheses is a good delegation target too. It is narrow and concrete: check whether the stated generic hypotheses really imply disjoint interior slope ranges in the two boundary cases.

Less good to delegate, but still possible:

audit-quintic-pure-value-trace can be delegated, but only if you frame it narrowly. It is subtle and easy for another chat to handwave. I would ask specifically whether trace annihilates the quintic pure-value slice under your exact normalization setup, or whether a different quotient detector is needed.

audit-direct-septic-witness can also be delegated if you ask very specifically for the graded-slice coefficient check and whether the direct q(7)q(7)-driven II-slice is generically nonzero.

Poor delegation targets:

audit-full-septic-fixed-sector is probably not a good one to delegate unless you hand over the exact order-7 sector decomposition and ask for explicit bookkeeping. Otherwise another chat will likely speculate.

audit-tangent-separator-sign-regularity is the least suitable to delegate as a vague task. It is now the real remaining theorem, and another chat is likely either to overclaim or to just restate the problem. It is fine to delegate a sub-piece of it, but not the whole thing in one shot.

Can there exist a first cross-interval zero whose common tangent occurs exactly at the boundary slope endpoint?‚Äã

That is subtler than the interior-slope version.

This is the one place where I still see a live issue.

If the first zero forces a common tangent at a slope that lies in the interior of both slope ranges, then disjoint slope interiors kill it. Good.

But if the common tangent occurs at the boundary endpoint slope, you need a bit more: you need to show that such an endpoint tangent from the right interval cannot hit the interior of the left interval.

That is exactly the boundary-support statement we were trying to prove earlier.

So the final theorem now seems to split into two subcases:

    interior tangent case ‚Äî ruled out by disjoint slope interiors;

    boundary endpoint tangent case ‚Äî still needs support separation.

That is a useful sharpening. It tells us the remaining global problem is smaller than ‚Äúall adjacent sign regularity.‚Äù It is just:

Can the endpoint tangent of the right interval hit the interior of the adjacent left interval?‚Äã

That is a one-sided support theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Endpoint tangent exclusion at an adjacent generic boundary]
\label{lem:endpoint-tangent-exclusion}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a boundary point \(h_b\),
and assume one of the boundary hypotheses of
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation} holds.

Then the boundary tangent line at \(\Gamma(h_b)\) meets neither adjacent
interval in its interior.

More precisely:

\begin{enumerate}
\item if \(W(h_b)=0\), \(M(h_b)\neq 0\), and
\[
m(h):=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
extends continuously across \(h_b\), then the affine tangent line of slope
\(m(h_b)\) at \(\Gamma(h_b)\) does not meet the interior of \(I_1\) or \(I_2\);

\item if \(M(h_b)=0\), \(M'(h_b)\neq 0\), and \(N(h_b)\neq 0\), then the
boundary tangent line is vertical and meets neither adjacent interval in its
interior.
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
In case (1), put
\[
m_b:=m(h_b),
\qquad
\phi_b(h):=\rho_2(h)-m_b\,\rho_1(h),
\]
where
\[
\rho_1(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
\rho_2(h)=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
Then
\[
\phi_b'(h)=\rho_1'(h)\bigl(m(h)-m_b\bigr).
\]
On each adjacent interval, \(\rho_1'\) has fixed sign because \(M\neq 0\), and
\(m(h)-m_b\) has fixed sign because \(m\) is strictly monotone there and
\(m_b\) is the boundary endpoint value. Hence \(\phi_b\) is strictly monotone
on each adjacent interval. The boundary tangent line is exactly the level set
\[
\phi_b(h)=\phi_b(h_b),
\]
so that level can be attained only at the boundary point itself, never in the
interior of \(I_1\) or \(I_2\).

In case (2), the tangent line is vertical:
\[
x=\rho_1(h_b).
\]
Since \(M(h_b)=0\) is a simple zero, \(\rho_1'(h_b)=0\) and
\(\rho_1''(h_b)\neq 0\), so \(\rho_1\) has a strict local extremum at
\(h_b\). Therefore
\[
\rho_1(h)-\rho_1(h_b)
\]
has fixed sign on each adjacent interval and vanishes only at the boundary
point. Hence the vertical tangent line meets neither adjacent interval in its
interior.
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity under generic boundary hypotheses]
\label{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals, and assume their common boundary
point \(h_b\) satisfies one of the generic boundary hypotheses of
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}. Then
\[
K(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1),
\]
where
\[
K(s,t)=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]
Equivalently, tangent lines from the right interval do not meet the adjacent
left interval.
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Suppose there exist \(s_0\in I_2\), \(t_0\in I_1\) with
\[
K(s_0,t_0)=0.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:endpoint-tangent-exclusion}, there is a neighborhood of the
boundary pair on which \(K(s,t)\neq 0\) for \(s\in I_2\), \(t\in I_1\). Hence,
after truncating to compact subintervals
\[
J_1\Subset I_1,\qquad J_2\Subset I_2
\]
containing all possible zeros, Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-zero-reduction}
produces a zero \((s_*,t_*)\in J_2\times J_1\) with minimal \(s\)-coordinate
in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering.

By Lemma~\ref{lem:first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency},
\[
K(s_*,t_*)=0,
\qquad
\partial_tK(s_*,t_*)=0,
\]
so the tangent line at \(\Gamma(s_*)\) is also tangent at \(\Gamma(t_*)\).

If both tangencies are interior tangencies, then the adjacent intervals share a
common interior tangent slope, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}.

If one tangency occurs at the boundary endpoint slope, this contradicts
Lemma~\ref{lem:endpoint-tangent-exclusion}, which rules out interior
intersections with the boundary tangent line on the adjacent interval.

Thus both possibilities are impossible, so no such zero \((s,t)\) exists.
Hence
\[
K(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1).
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Global no-secant and no-four-secant theorem]
\label{thm:global-no-secant}
Assume every adjacent pair of ECT intervals satisfies the generic boundary
hypotheses of Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}. Then no
affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals. In particular, there is no
global four-secant line for the normalized planar curve \(\Gamma(h)\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals. A tangent-support line to
\(\Gamma(I_2)\) at \(\Gamma(s)\), \(s\in I_2\), meets \(\Gamma(I_1)\) exactly
at the zeros of
\[
t\longmapsto K(s,t).
\]
By Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}, no such zero
exists. Therefore \(\Gamma(I_1)\) lies strictly on one side of every
tangent-support line of \(\Gamma(I_2)\), so the support intervals of adjacent
ECT branches are disjoint.

By transitivity over the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering, support intervals of any two
distinct ECT intervals are disjoint. Hence no affine line can be \(2\)-secant
to two different ECT intervals, and no global four-secant line exists.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Exclusion of pair-pair branch configurations]
\label{cor:pair-pair-exclusion}
Under the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:global-no-secant}, a bad finite core
cannot contain two distinct ECT intervals each contributing a pair of points.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Each ECT interval contributes at most two points to the zero set of an affine
line in the normalized chart, and by
Theorem~\ref{thm:global-no-secant} no affine line can be \(2\)-secant to two
different ECT intervals. Therefore a pair-pair configuration across two
distinct ECT intervals is impossible.
\end{proof}

So my updated judgment is:

    Adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity: plausibly in good shape, modulo its own audit.

    Global no-secant / no-4-secant as currently written: not airtight yet.

    The missing piece is a real lemma, not just wording.

    prove an adjacent support-ordering lemma:
    I1<I2‚üπBI1(m)‚à©BI2(m)=‚àÖ
    I1‚Äã<I2‚Äã‚üπBI1‚Äã‚Äã(m)‚à©BI2‚Äã‚Äã(m)=‚àÖ

    then deduce the global no-secant theorem by transitivity over adjacent intervals.

1. Insert the convex-analysis lemmas

\begin{theorem}[Global no-secant and no-four-secant theorem]
\label{thm:global-no-secant}

Insert this exact LaTeX

\begin{lemma}[Fixed-slope intercept sets for a strictly convex ECT branch]
\label{lem:fixed-slope-intercept-sets}
Let \(I\) be an ECT interval in the affine chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c},
\]
and suppose \(\Gamma(I)\) is a strictly convex \(C^2\) graph
\[
\Gamma(I)=\{(x,\psi_I(x)):x\in J_I\}
\]
over an open interval \(J_I\subset\mathbf R\).

For each slope \(m\in\mathbf R\), define
\[
\mathcal B_I(m):=\{\psi_I(x)-mx:x\in J_I\}.
\]

Then:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(\mathcal B_I(m)\) is an interval.

\item A line \(y=mx+b\) meets \(\Gamma(I)\) if and only if
\[
b\in \mathcal B_I(m).
\]

\item If \(m\) lies in the interior tangent-slope range of \(I\), there is a
unique \(x_I(m)\in J_I\) such that
\[
\psi_I'(x_I(m))=m,
\]
and the tangent line of slope \(m\) has intercept
\[
\tau_I(m):=\psi_I(x_I(m))-m\,x_I(m).
\]
Moreover,
\[
\tau_I(m)=\inf \mathcal B_I(m).
\]

\item For such \(m\), a line \(y=mx+b\) is \(2\)-secant to \(\Gamma(I)\) if and
only if
\[
b\in \operatorname{int}\mathcal B_I(m).
\]
Equivalently, the \(2\)-secant intercepts of slope \(m\) are exactly the
interior points of the intercept interval.
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
For fixed \(m\), put
\[
\phi_m(x):=\psi_I(x)-mx.
\]
Since \(\phi_m\) is continuous on the interval \(J_I\), its image
\[
\phi_m(J_I)=\mathcal B_I(m)
\]
is an interval. This proves (1), and (2) is immediate from
\[
(x,\psi_I(x))\in \{y=mx+b\}
\iff
\psi_I(x)-mx=b.
\]

If \(m\) lies in the interior tangent-slope range, strict convexity implies
that \(\psi_I'\) is strictly monotone, hence there is a unique \(x_I(m)\)
with \(\psi_I'(x_I(m))=m\). At that point,
\[
\phi_m'(x_I(m))=\psi_I'(x_I(m))-m=0,
\]
and since
\[
\phi_m''(x)=\psi_I''(x)>0,
\]
the point \(x_I(m)\) is the unique minimizer of \(\phi_m\). Thus
\[
\tau_I(m)=\phi_m(x_I(m))=\inf \mathcal B_I(m),
\]
which proves (3).

Finally, because \(\phi_m\) is strictly convex with a unique minimum,
a level \(b\) is attained at two points exactly when it lies in the interior
of the interval \(\phi_m(J_I)=\mathcal B_I(m)\). This is precisely the
\(2\)-secant condition, proving (4).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Adjacent support-ordering]
\label{lem:adjacent-support-ordering}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals under the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}. Then, for each
slope \(m\) lying in the common tangent-slope range of the two branches, the
fixed-slope intercept intervals satisfy
\[
\mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\cap \mathcal B_{I_2}(m)=\varnothing.
\]
More precisely,
\[
\sup \mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\le \inf \mathcal B_{I_2}(m).
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Fix such a slope \(m\). Let \(s\in I_2\) be the unique point with tangent slope
\(m\), so the tangent line to \(\Gamma(I_2)\) at \(\Gamma(s)\) has intercept
\[
\tau_{I_2}(m)=\inf \mathcal B_{I_2}(m)
\]
by Lemma~\ref{lem:fixed-slope-intercept-sets}.

By Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}, this tangent
line does not meet \(\Gamma(I_1)\). Therefore for every \(t\in I_1\),
\[
\psi_{I_1}(x(t)) - m\,x(t) \le \tau_{I_2}(m),
\]
so
\[
\sup \mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\le \tau_{I_2}(m)=\inf \mathcal B_{I_2}(m).
\]
Hence the intercept intervals are disjoint.
\end{proof}

2. Replace the proof of the global no-secant theorem
Find the current proof of

\begin{theorem}[Global no-secant and no-four-secant theorem]
\label{thm:global-no-secant}
...
\end{theorem}

Replace only the \begin{proof} ... \end{proof} block with:

\begin{proof}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals. By
Lemma~\ref{lem:adjacent-support-ordering}, for every slope \(m\) in the common
tangent-slope range, the fixed-slope intercept intervals are disjoint:
\[
\mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\cap \mathcal B_{I_2}(m)=\varnothing.
\]
Hence no affine line of slope \(m\) can be \(2\)-secant to both adjacent
branches.

Now let \(I_a<I_b\) be any two distinct ECT intervals. By iterating the
adjacent support-ordering inequality across the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering, one gets
disjointness of the corresponding fixed-slope intercept intervals for \(I_a\)
and \(I_b\) as well. Therefore no affine line can be \(2\)-secant to two
different ECT intervals.

In particular, a global four-secant line would have to be \(2\)-secant to two
distinct ECT intervals, which is impossible.
\end{proof}

So after this patch, the global no-secant theorem is no longer leaning on an undefined convex-analysis bridge.

I checked the latest, it is better, but I do **not** think the global no-secant theorem is fully closed yet.

The good part first: the new lemma lem:fixed-slope-intercept-sets is fine. Its four claims are the right convex-graph facts, and it is a useful ingredient.

The trouble is in the next two steps.

In lem:adjacent-support-ordering, the sentence

[
\psi_{I_1}(x(t)) - m x(t) \le \tau_{I_2}(m)
]

does **not** follow just from ‚Äúthe tangent line does not meet (\Gamma(I_1)).‚Äù Nonintersection only gives that
[
\psi_{I_1}(x(t)) - m x(t) - \tau_{I_2}(m)
]
has constant nonzero sign on (I_1). It could be all (<0) or all (>0). The proof currently chooses the (\le) direction without justification.

That is a real gap, but now it is a very specific one, and there is a clean repair. For (s\in I_2), if (m) is the tangent slope at (s), then in the affine chart
[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c},
]
one has the exact identity
[
K(s,t)
======

c(s)^2 c(t),x'(s)\Big((y(t)-m x(t))-(y(s)-m x(s))\Big).
]
Equivalently,
[
K(s,t)
======

c(s)^2 c(t),x'(s)\Big((y(t)-m x(t))-\tau_{I_2}(m)\Big).
]
So once you know the sign of (K) on (I_2\times I_1), you know on which side of the tangent line the left branch lies. The adjacent theorem already gives (K\neq 0), and continuity gives constant sign on the rectangle. What is still missing is one short argument fixing which sign occurs. That can be done by evaluating near the boundary, where the endpoint tangent exclusion tells you which side the adjacent branch occupies.

So lem:adjacent-support-ordering is close, but not yet proved as written.

Then there is a second issue in thm:global-no-secant. The line

> ‚ÄúBy iterating the adjacent support-ordering inequality across the (x=v_5/c) ordering ...‚Äù

is too fast. As written, the adjacent-support-ordering lemma only applies for slopes (m) in the **common tangent-slope range** of the adjacent pair. But in the chain from (I_a) to (I_b), the chosen slope (m) need not lie in the tangent-slope range of every intermediate interval. So the current iteration step is not justified.

That means the present status is:

* lem:fixed-slope-intercept-sets: okay.
* lem:adjacent-support-ordering: still has a real sign-direction gap.
* thm:global-no-secant: still has that gap, plus an additional iteration gap.

So the newest version is improved, but I would not yet treat the global theorem as finished.

The clean repair path is:

1. add the affine identity for (K(s,t)) and use it to fix the side in lem:adjacent-support-ordering;
2. strengthen that lemma so it orders intercept sets for adjacent branches for any slope (m) for which both intercept sets are nonempty, not only slopes in the common tangent-slope range;
3. then the transitive step in the global theorem becomes legitimate.

1. Insert the affine identity for K(s,t)K(s,t)
Where to insert

Insert this immediately after

Insert this exact LaTeX

\begin{lemma}[Affine form of the tangent-separator kernel]
\label{lem:affine-form-of-K}
On the affine chart \(c\neq 0\), write
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
Then for every \(s,t\) with \(c(s)c(t)\neq 0\),
\[
K(s,t)
=
c(s)^2\,c(t)\,x'(s)
\Bigl(
\bigl(y(t)-m(s)x(t)\bigr)
-
\bigl(y(s)-m(s)x(s)\bigr)
\Bigr),
\]
where
\[
m(s)=\frac{dy}{dx}(s)=\frac{N(s)}{M(s)}.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
K(s,t)
=
c(s)^2\,c(t)\,x'(s)\,
\bigl(\phi_{m(s)}(t)-\tau_s\bigr),
\]
with
\[
\phi_{m(s)}(t):=y(t)-m(s)x(t),
\qquad
\tau_s:=y(s)-m(s)x(s).
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Write
\[
F(h)=\bigl(c(h),\,c(h)x(h),\,c(h)y(h)\bigr).
\]
Then
\[
F'(s)=\bigl(c'(s),\,c'(s)x(s)+c(s)x'(s),\,c'(s)y(s)+c(s)y'(s)\bigr).
\]
Subtracting \(x(s)\) times the first column from the second and \(y(s)\) times
the first column from the third gives
\[
K(s,t)
=
\det\!
\begin{pmatrix}
c(s) & 0 & 0\\
c'(s) & c(s)x'(s) & c(s)y'(s)\\
c(t) & c(t)(x(t)-x(s)) & c(t)(y(t)-y(s))
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Expanding along the first row yields
\[
K(s,t)
=
c(s)^2c(t)\Bigl(x'(s)(y(t)-y(s))-y'(s)(x(t)-x(s))\Bigr).
\]
Since
\[
m(s)=\frac{y'(s)}{x'(s)},
\]
this becomes
\[
K(s,t)
=
c(s)^2c(t)x'(s)
\Bigl((y(t)-m(s)x(t))-(y(s)-m(s)x(s))\Bigr),
\]
as claimed.
\end{proof}

2. Replace lem:adjacent-support-ordering

The current version is too weak and chooses a sign without justification.
Find

\begin{lemma}[Adjacent support-ordering]

Replace the entire block with

\begin{lemma}[Adjacent support-ordering]
\label{lem:adjacent-support-ordering}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals under the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}. Then, for every
slope \(m\in\mathbf R\) such that both fixed-slope intercept sets are nonempty,
\[
\mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\cap \mathcal B_{I_2}(m)=\varnothing.
\]
More precisely, one has one of the two strict alternatives
\[
\sup \mathcal B_{I_1}(m)<\inf \mathcal B_{I_2}(m)
\qquad\text{or}\qquad
\sup \mathcal B_{I_2}(m)<\inf \mathcal B_{I_1}(m),
\]
and the same alternative holds for all such \(m\) on the adjacent pair.
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Fix \(m\) such that both \(\mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\) and \(\mathcal B_{I_2}(m)\)
are nonempty.

First assume \(m\) lies in the interior tangent-slope range of \(I_2\). Let
\(s\in I_2\) be the unique point with tangent slope \(m\). Then by
Lemma~\ref{lem:fixed-slope-intercept-sets},
\[
\tau_{I_2}(m)=\inf \mathcal B_{I_2}(m).
\]
By Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity},
\[
K(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad (t\in I_1).
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:affine-form-of-K},
\[
K(s,t)=c(s)^2c(t)x'(s)\bigl(\phi_m(t)-\tau_{I_2}(m)\bigr),
\qquad
\phi_m(t):=y(t)-mx(t).
\]
Since \(c(s)^2c(t)x'(s)\) has fixed sign on \(I_1\) and \(K(s,t)\) is continuous
and nowhere zero there, the quantity
\[
\phi_m(t)-\tau_{I_2}(m)
\]
has constant sign on \(I_1\). To determine which sign occurs, move \(s\) toward
the common boundary point from inside \(I_2\). By
Lemma~\ref{lem:endpoint-tangent-exclusion}, the limiting boundary tangent line
does not meet \(I_1\), so \(I_1\) lies entirely on one side of that tangent
line. By continuity, the same side persists for nearby interior tangents, hence
for all \(s\in I_2\). Therefore either
\[
\phi_m(t)<\tau_{I_2}(m)\quad\text{for all }t\in I_1,
\]
or
\[
\phi_m(t)>\tau_{I_2}(m)\quad\text{for all }t\in I_1.
\]
In the first case,
\[
\sup\mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\le \tau_{I_2}(m)=\inf\mathcal B_{I_2}(m),
\]
and since equality would force an intersection with the tangent line, the
inequality is strict. The second case is symmetric.

Now suppose \(m\) is not in the interior tangent-slope range of \(I_2\), but
both intercept sets are nonempty. Then \(m\) lies on one side of the tangent
slope range of \(I_2\), so \(\phi_m\) is strictly monotone on \(I_2\), and
\(\mathcal B_{I_2}(m)\) is an interval with endpoint attained in the one-sided
limit at the adjacent boundary. Passing to that boundary-support line and using
Lemma~\ref{lem:endpoint-tangent-exclusion} again shows that \(I_1\) lies
strictly on one side of that boundary-support line. Hence the same strict
ordering of intercept sets holds in this case as well.

Thus \(\mathcal B_{I_1}(m)\) and \(\mathcal B_{I_2}(m)\) are disjoint for every
slope \(m\) for which both are nonempty, and the side ordering is constant on
the adjacent pair.
\end{proof}

3. Replace the proof of thm:global-no-secant

Now that the adjacent-support-ordering lemma is strong enough, the iteration is valid.
Find the proof of

\begin{theorem}[Global no-secant and no-four-secant theorem]
\label{thm:global-no-secant}
...
\end{theorem}

Replace only the proof with

\begin{proof}
Let \(I_a<I_b\) be two distinct ECT intervals, and let \(m\in\mathbf R\) be a
slope such that both fixed-slope intercept sets
\[
\mathcal B_{I_a}(m),\qquad \mathcal B_{I_b}(m)
\]
are nonempty. Enumerate the adjacent ECT intervals between them as
\[
I_a=J_0<J_1<\cdots<J_r=I_b.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:adjacent-support-ordering}, for each adjacent pair
\(J_{k}<J_{k+1}\), the fixed-slope intercept sets are disjoint, with a
consistent ordering:
\[
\sup \mathcal B_{J_k}(m)<\inf \mathcal B_{J_{k+1}}(m)
\qquad (k=0,\dots,r-1).
\]
Iterating these inequalities gives
\[
\sup \mathcal B_{I_a}(m)<\inf \mathcal B_{I_b}(m).
\]
Hence
\[
\mathcal B_{I_a}(m)\cap \mathcal B_{I_b}(m)=\varnothing.
\]

By Lemma~\ref{lem:fixed-slope-intercept-sets}, a line \(y=mx+b\) is \(2\)-secant
to \(\Gamma(I)\) only if \(b\in \operatorname{int}\mathcal B_I(m)\). Therefore
no affine line can be \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals.

In particular, a global four-secant line would have to be \(2\)-secant to two
distinct ECT intervals, which is impossible.
\end{proof}

The current theorem statement is probably fine, but if you want it to track the strengthened lemma better, replace the sentence

Then no affine line in the chart ... is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals.

with

Then for any two distinct ECT intervals \(I_a\neq I_b\), and any affine line in
the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c},
\]
that line cannot be \(2\)-secant to both \(\Gamma(I_a)\) and \(\Gamma(I_b)\).

This is optional.

The revised lem:adjacent-support-ordering now uses one more subtle continuity statement:

    the side of the tangent line occupied by the adjacent branch is determined near the boundary and persists for all interior tangents.

I think that is the right repair, but it is now the one place a referee might still press. It is much smaller than the previous gap, though. The theorem is no longer missing the whole convex-analysis layer.

So this patch genuinely addresses the critique:

how can we referee proof this?

To make this referee-proof, I would stop trying to ‚Äúsell the whole picture‚Äù and instead turn the remaining geometry into a short chain of tiny, brutal lemmas with no hidden transitions.

For the current global layer, the referee-sensitive point is exactly what the comment identified: the proof is only convincing if every jump from tangent-separator to support ordering to global no-secant is formalized, with no undefined phrases like ‚Äúsupport interval‚Äù and no silent sign choices.

The way to referee-proof it is this.

First, freeze the objects once and for all. Define, in one place:
F(h)=(c(h),v5(h),Œî7(h)),x(h)=v5(h)c(h),y(h)=Œî7(h)c(h),
F(h)=(c(h),v5‚Äã(h),Œî7‚Äã(h)),x(h)=c(h)v5‚Äã(h)‚Äã,y(h)=c(h)Œî7‚Äã(h)‚Äã,
K(s,t)=det‚Å°(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)).
K(s,t)=det(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)).

Then prove the affine identity
K(s,t)=c(s)2c(t)x‚Ä≤(s)((y(t)‚àím(s)x(t))‚àí(y(s)‚àím(s)x(s))),
K(s,t)=c(s)2c(t)x‚Ä≤(s)((y(t)‚àím(s)x(t))‚àí(y(s)‚àím(s)x(s))),

with
m(s)=dydx(s).
m(s)=dxdy‚Äã(s).

That identity is the real bridge. Without it, the sign of KK does not transparently tell you which side of the tangent line the other branch lies on.

Second, separate the global theorem into three lemmas.

One: fixed-slope intercept sets for a strictly convex branch. This should define
BI(m)={y‚àímx:(x,y)‚ààŒì(I)}
BI‚Äã(m)={y‚àímx:(x,y)‚ààŒì(I)}

and prove:

    it is an interval,

    a line y=mx+by=mx+b meets the branch iff b‚ààBI(m)b‚ààBI‚Äã(m),

    the tangent intercept is an endpoint of BI(m)BI‚Äã(m),

    22-secancy corresponds to b‚ààint‚Å°BI(m)b‚ààintBI‚Äã(m).

Two: adjacent support ordering. This is the one that must be stated in the right strength. Not ‚Äúfor slopes in the common tangent-slope range,‚Äù but:

    for any slope mm such that both BI1(m)BI1‚Äã‚Äã(m) and BI2(m)BI2‚Äã‚Äã(m) are nonempty, the two intervals are disjoint, with a definite order.

That stronger form is what you need for the transitive global proof. The comment is right that the weaker version is not enough.

Three: global transitivity. Once adjacent support ordering is proved in that strengthened form, the global no-secant theorem becomes one line:
Ia=J0<J1<‚ãØ<Jr=Ib
Ia‚Äã=J0‚Äã<J1‚Äã<‚ãØ<Jr‚Äã=Ib‚Äã

and
sup‚Å°BJk(m)<inf‚Å°BJk+1(m)
supBJk‚Äã‚Äã(m)<infBJk+1‚Äã‚Äã(m)

for each adjacent pair, hence
sup‚Å°BIa(m)<inf‚Å°BIb(m).
supBIa‚Äã‚Äã(m)<infBIb‚Äã‚Äã(m).

Then no line can be 22-secant to both.

Third, isolate the ‚Äúfirst zero‚Äù argument as a standalone local theorem and do not bury it in prose. A referee will attack exactly these points:

    how the ‚Äúfirst zero‚Äù exists,

    why it cannot be transverse,

    why K=0K=0 and ‚àÇtK=0‚àÇt‚ÄãK=0 really means common tangency,

    which generic boundary hypotheses are needed for disjoint adjacent slope interiors.

So make that a sequence:

    compact truncation lemma,

    first-zero-implies-‚àÇtK=0‚àÇt‚ÄãK=0,

    common tangent interpretation,

    adjacent slope-range separation proposition,

    endpoint tangent exclusion lemma.

Then your adjacent sign theorem should read like a theorem assembled from those pieces, not like a narrative argument.

Fourth, stop overstating anything that is still conditional. Right now the remaining items that are still not referee-proof are local/algebraic:

    first-order surjectivity,

    quintic pure-value trace annihilation or replacement detector,

    full septic fixed-sector completion,

    direct septic witness at the full-total level.

Those should stay explicitly audited. Referees are much happier with a paper that is honest about what remains than with one that sounds finished but has one slippery step.

Fifth, add a dependency map right before the endgame. One short remark listing:

    what is proved unconditionally,

    what is proved under generic boundary hypotheses,

    what still depends on audited local inputs.

That makes it much easier for a referee to see the architecture and much harder for them to say ‚Äúthe proof is impossible to audit.‚Äù

If you want the shortest actionable checklist, it is:

    prove the affine identity for K(s,t)K(s,t);

    strengthen adjacent support ordering to all slopes with both intercept sets nonempty;

    rewrite global no-secant using only those intercept lemmas;

    isolate first-zero/common-tangent as standalone lemmas;

    keep the remaining local algebra under explicit audit labels.

That is how I would try to make this referee-proof.

I think that critique is basically correct.

The counterexample matters. It shows that from the abstract package

    strictly convex ECT branches,

What I think survives from that route is the first reduction lemma:

That is a good and correct reduction. It sharpens the obstruction from ‚Äúcommon 2-secant‚Äù to ‚Äútangent-hit.‚Äù The other chat is right that this part is worth keeping.

Where I now agree the argument fails is the next step:

    ‚Äúminimal-gap tangent-hit must be adjacent‚Äù

That is false in general convex geometry, and their three-branch counterexample makes the point cleanly. So if the current draft is trying to deduce global no-secant from only adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity, then yes, there is still a real gap.

My updated view is:
What is still valid

    the normalized curve Œì=(v5/c,Œî7/c)Œì=(v5‚Äã/c,Œî7‚Äã/c) setup,

    the projective Wronskian / ECT interval layer,

    the reduction ‚Äúcommon 2-secant ‚áí‚áí tangent-hit.‚Äù

What is not yet justified

    global no-secant from adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity alone.

So the real remaining geometric theorem is stronger than what I was using. It is something like:
For each fixed slope m, the intercept intervals BI(m) are globally ordered by branch order.
For each fixed slope m, the intercept intervals BI‚Äã(m) are globally ordered by branch order.‚Äã

Concretely, for branches I<JI<J, whenever both intercept sets are nonempty, one wants
sup‚Å°BI(m)<inf‚Å°BJ(m),
supBI‚Äã(m)<infBJ‚Äã(m),

with consistent orientation.

That would really imply global no-secant. And it is stronger than adjacent tangent exclusion.

So my honest conclusion is:

    the other chat successfully found a real structural gap in the claimed global closure;

    the global no-secant theorem should be treated as still open unless you prove a stronger fixed-slope global ordering theorem;

    the valid part to keep is the reduction lemma from common secant to tangent-hit.

If I were steering the draft now, I would do one of two things:

    Conservative: downgrade the current global no-secant theorem back to a conditional statement, and keep the tangent-hit reduction as the sharp current endpoint.

    Aggressive: try to prove a genuinely stronger global ordering theorem for the special triple (c,v5,Œî7)(c,v5‚Äã,Œî7‚Äã), not for arbitrary convex branches. That would have to use more than convexity ‚Äî likely some total positivity / variation-diminishing / sign-regularity property of the determinant kernel tied to the special origin of the curve.

So: I think the criticism is right, and it changes the status of the global endgame. The geometry is still much better organized than before, but the final ‚Äúglobal no-secant‚Äù closure is not yet proved from the present ingredients.

let's go aggressive--let's attack it

I‚Äôm going aggressive, but with one constraint: we now know plain convexity is not enough, so the next attack has to exploit special structure of the triple (c,v5,Œî7)(c,v5‚Äã,Œî7‚Äã), not just generic branch geometry. The right candidates are total positivity / sign-regularity of determinant kernels, or a stronger one-parameter Chebyshev property across branch decompositions.

Good. Then we should stop trying to win with generic convexity and attack the special kernel structure of
F(h)=(c(h),v5(h),Œî7(h)).
F(h)=(c(h),v5‚Äã(h),Œî7‚Äã(h)).

The other chat‚Äôs counterexample kills any route that uses only:

    convex ECT branches,

    xx-ordering,

    adjacent tangent exclusion.

So the aggressive route has to prove a property that generic convex triples do not have.

The right candidate is a sign-regular / total-positivity-type statement for the determinant kernel built from FF.
1. The right kernel to attack

There are really two kernels now:

The tangent kernel
K(s,t):=det‚Å°(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)),
K(s,t):=det(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)),

and the secant kernel
D(s,t,u):=det‚Å°(F(s),F(t),F(u)).
D(s,t,u):=det(F(s),F(t),F(u)).

Why DD matters:
a line in the affine chart
x=v5c,y=Œî7c
x=cv5‚Äã‚Äã,y=cŒî7‚Äã‚Äã

corresponds to a plane through the origin in the projective picture of FF. So multiple intersections with one affine line are exactly multiple parameters whose FF-vectors lie in the same 2-plane, i.e. whose 3-by-3 determinant vanishes.

So a 4-secant is really about repeated vanishing of
D(h1,h2,h3)=0
D(h1‚Äã,h2‚Äã,h3‚Äã)=0

for different triples on one common plane.

This suggests the genuinely stronger theorem we need is not about convexity of each branch, but about ordered sign behavior of
D(s,t,u)=det‚Å°(F(s),F(t),F(u)).
D(s,t,u)=det(F(s),F(t),F(u)).

If we could prove:
D(s,t,u) has a fixed sign whenever s<t<u in the global parameter order on the generic region,
D(s,t,u) has a fixed sign whenever s<t<u in the global parameter order on the generic region,‚Äã

then the whole thing would collapse.

Because then no three distinct ordered points could be collinear in the affine chart at all. That would be stronger than no-4-secant; it would imply no 3-secant.

That is probably too strong globally. But it tells us what to look for: variation-diminishing / sign-regularity of the 3-point determinant.
2. A weaker but still sufficient aggressive target

We probably do not need full sign-regularity of DD for all ordered triples.

It is enough to show something like:
For each fixed pair s<t,  u‚Ü¶D(s,t,u) has at most one zero outside [s,t].
For each fixed pair s<t,  u‚Ü¶D(s,t,u) has at most one zero outside [s,t].‚Äã

Why this would be enough:

    if one line were 22-secant to two distant branches, then fixing two points on one branch would force the determinant to vanish at two distinct points on the other side;

    that would violate the one-zero-outside-interval property.

This is much more plausible than full global sign-regularity, and it is already stronger than the current adjacent tangent theorem.

So the aggressive theorem becomes:

    Two-point variation theorem: for fixed s<ts<t, the function
    u‚Ü¶D(s,t,u)
    u‚Ü¶D(s,t,u)

    is Chebyshev-like, with at most one zero on each connected component of the complement of [s,t][s,t].

That would kill pair-pair configurations.
3. Why this might actually be true here

Now we use special structure.

Write
F(h)=(c(h),v5(h),Œî7(h)).
F(h)=(c(h),v5‚Äã(h),Œî7‚Äã(h)).

The key derived fact is that
W(h)=det‚Å°(F,F‚Ä≤,F‚Ä≤‚Ä≤)
W(h)=det(F,F‚Ä≤,F‚Ä≤‚Ä≤)

1. Replace the global no-secant theorem stack with conditional / partial statements
Find this theorem

\begin{theorem}[Global no-secant and no-four-secant theorem]
\label{thm:global-no-secant}
...
\end{theorem}

Replace the entire theorem with this

\begin{theorem}[Partial global no-secant theorem away from \(W=0\) boundaries]
\label{thm:partial-global-no-secant}
Assume every adjacent pair of ECT intervals is separated by a generic turning
boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
and that no adjacent pair is separated by a boundary of type
\[
W(h_b)=0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Then no affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals. In particular, there is no
global four-secant line for the normalized planar curve \(\Gamma(h)\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Under the stated hypotheses, every adjacent boundary is of turning-point type.
For such adjacent pairs, Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}
and Lemma~\ref{lem:adjacent-support-ordering} apply without encountering the
generic \(W=0\) inflection obstruction. Hence adjacent fixed-slope intercept
sets are strictly ordered. Iterating this ordering over the \(x=v_5/c\)
ordering of ECT intervals shows that no affine line can be \(2\)-secant to two
distinct ECT intervals. Therefore no global four-secant line exists.
\end{proof}

Find this corollary

\begin{corollary}[Exclusion of pair-pair branch configurations]
\label{cor:pair-pair-exclusion}
...
\end{corollary}

Replace it with

\begin{corollary}[Partial exclusion of pair-pair branch configurations]
\label{cor:pair-pair-exclusion}
Under the hypotheses of Theorem~\ref{thm:partial-global-no-secant}, a bad
finite core cannot contain two distinct ECT intervals each contributing a pair
of points.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
Each ECT interval contributes at most two points to the zero set of an affine
line in the normalized chart, and by
Theorem~\ref{thm:partial-global-no-secant} no affine line can be \(2\)-secant
to two different ECT intervals. Therefore a pair-pair configuration across two
distinct ECT intervals is impossible.
\end{proof}

2. Insert the new W=0W=0 obstruction remark
Where to insert

Insert this immediately after

\begin{theorem}[Adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity under generic boundary hypotheses]
\label{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}

\begin{remark}[Failure of the tangent-separator route at generic \(W=0\) boundaries]
\label{rem:w0-inflection-obstruction}
The preceding adjacent tangent-separator argument is robust at generic turning
boundaries
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
but does not automatically extend across generic boundaries of type
\[
W(h_b)=0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
In the affine chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},\qquad y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c},
\]
such a boundary is generically modeled by an ordinary affine inflection
\[
y=ax^3+O(x^4),\qquad a\neq 0,
\]
and the tangent line at a point \(x=s\) on one side of the inflection meets the
opposite side again at \(x=-2s\) in the cubic model. Thus adjacent
tangent-separator sign regularity, and hence the global no-secant theorem, are
not presently justified across generic \(W=0\), \(M\neq 0\) boundaries without
additional special structure of the actual triple \((c,v_5,\Delta_7)\).
\end{remark}

\begin{remark}[Current state of the argument]
\label{rem:current-state-of-argument}
The local one-pair analysis now consists of three layers:
the cubic leading scalar \(c(h)\), the quintic fixed-sector refinement
\(A_5^{\mathfrak f}(h)\), and the septic determinant \(\Delta_7(h)\).
These determine the normalized planar curve
\[
\Gamma(h)=\left(\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}\right),
\]
together with the derivative numerators
\[
M(h)=c(h)v_5'(h)-c'(h)v_5(h),
\qquad
N(h)=c(h)\Delta_7'(h)-c'(h)\Delta_7(h),
\]
and the projective Wronskian
\[
W(h)=
\det\!
\begin{pmatrix}
c&v_5&\Delta_7\\
c'&v_5'&\Delta_7'\\
c''&v_5''&\Delta_7''
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

On intervals where \(c\), \(M\), and \(W\) do not vanish, the triple
\((c,v_5,\Delta_7)\) is an ECT system. The turning-point boundaries

M=0,\qquad M'\neq 0,\qquad N\neq 0

behavior of \(\Gamma\) across boundaries of type

W=0,\qquad M\neq 0,
\]
where generic inflection geometry shows that the tangent-separator route does
not close without additional special structure. The remaining audited issues
are therefore both local and global: first-order surjectivity, the quintic
pure-value quotient detector, the completion of the order-\(7\) fixed-sector
algebra, and the special affine-jet structure of \(\Gamma\) at \(W=0\)
boundaries.
\end{remark}
